nEoJ|2-0J_	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Pupil as pinhole; photoreceptor; fovea; periphery	Dilating the pupil causes the image to become blurry<div>Both the cornea and the lens are lenses basically</div><div>Adjustable lens - ‚Öì of focusing power</div><div>When light hits the top of a photoreceptor, the output is graded based on the input</div><div>With only one photoreceptor present, Rods only read the intensity of light</div><div>3 kinds of cones: small (blues), medium (greens), large (red)</div><div>Non uniform retina tiling: small photoreceptor in center of gaze (fovea) where the optics produce the best image, and larger photoreceptors in the periphery</div><div>At each distance from the fovea the rods are smaller than the cones&nbsp;</div><div>Consequence: lower spatial resolution in the periphery</div><div>The fovea contains only cones</div><div>There are 100 million photoreceptors per retina</div><div>Compute and process 1.2 million worth with a neutral layer after the optic layer</div><div>There‚Äôs a blind spot in your visual field where the retina has no photoreceptors</div><div>Light enters through the ganglion cells, passes through bipolar/horizontal/amacrine cells, gets to photoreceptors and is transduced (computed), then travels back through the layers to the ganglion where it can be read by the brain as data</div><div>Fun: the size of dendritic trees of ganglion neurons increases with distance from the fovea</div><div>So the peripheral ganglion neurons sum information from more photoreceptors than the foveal neurons</div>	perception

hpxkTIyAdx	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Retina Functions	"<div>¬∑&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<u>Transduction</u>: transformation of light
energy to electrical signals</div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<div>Photoreceptors transform light
energy (photons) to electrical signals. There are two types of photoreceptors
(rods and cones) which both absorb photons and generate an electrical
potential. Thus, both rods and cones perform transduction.</div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<div>¬∑&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<img alt=""A picture containing circuit

Description automatically generated"" src=""clip_image001.png""><u>Dark adaptation</u>: The time it takes
photoreceptors to reach their maximal sensitivity in low light conditions</div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<div>Although
rods and cones both perform transduction, their sensitivity to the light is
different. As you can see from the figure on the right, rods have much lower
threshold than the cones. This suggests that rods are more sensitive than
cones, as transduction in rods occurs at lower light intensities than in cones.
Therefore, night vision is based on rods. However, as you might have noticed
from the ‚Äòtime in the dark (min)‚Äô, it takes rods longer time than cones to
reach maximal sensitivity. To be specific, for cones it takes about 8 mins to
reach the maximal sensitivity while, it takes about 20 mins for rods.</div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<div>¬∑&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<u>Wavelength encoding</u>: Different
photoreceptors have pigments that absorb light at different wavelengths,
forming the basis for color vision </div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<div>All rods have the same
photopigment (rhodopsin) while, cones have 3 kinds of photopigments, which
respond to long, medium, and short wavelengths. As we can see from the figure
below, each photoreceptor observes a range of wavelengths, not a single wavelength.&nbsp;</div><div><br></div><div><div>¬∑&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
&nbsp;<u>Data
compression: </u>why we see blurry in the periphery. </div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<div>Spatial distribution of photoreceptors
is not uniform across the retina. We have more cones in the fovea than
periphery. In contrast, we have more rods in the periphery (their density peaks
at round 20 deg) and have almost no rods in the fovea. Therefore, fovea is
dominated by the signals from the cones and periphery is dominated by the
signals of the rods.</div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<div>Many-to-one relationship is found
in the periphery. Inputs from as many as 50 photoreceptors converge to a single
ganglion cell. &nbsp;Pooling information from
multiple photoreceptors increases visual sensitivity (ability to detect/perceive
light), which makes rod system to function well in dim light conditions.
However, as the information is coming from multiple photoreceptors, the peripheral
vision has low visual acuity (the finest detail that can be resolved by the
eyes).</div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<div>Whereas, in the fovea, cones tend
to have one-to-one relationship with the ganglion cells. This one-to-one
relationship results in increase in visual acuity and decrease in sensitivity
to light. This is why we see clear images in the fovea and blurry images in the
periphery.</div>
<div><b>&nbsp;</b></div>
<div><img alt=""A close up of a map

Description automatically generated"" src=""clip_image001-f20de361fb24fa8d39eb522b09feb8f27dc814b3.png""></div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<u>Spatial filtering:</u> enhance edges (<i>see Receptivefields.mp4 on
Canvas)</i><br></div><div><br></div>"	perception

uIq7o0UE8r	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Place Coding vs Temporal Coding	"Place coding in Cochlea vs time coding in auditory nerve<br><br>The nature of pitch and its neural coding have been studied for over a 
century. A popular debate has revolved around the question of whether 
pitch is coded via ‚Äúplace‚Äù cues in the cochlea, or via timing cues in 
the auditory nerve. In the most recent incarnation of this debate, the 
role of temporal fine structure has been emphasized in conveying 
important pitch and speech information, particularly because the lack of
 temporal fine structure coding in cochlear implants might explain some 
of the difficulties faced by cochlear implant users in perceiving music 
and pitch contours in speech. In addition, some studies have postulated 
that hearing-impaired listeners may have a specific deficit related to 
processing temporal fine structure. This article reviews some of the 
recent literature surrounding the debate, and argues that much of the 
recent evidence suggesting the importance of temporal fine structure 
processing can also be accounted for using spectral (place) or 
temporal-envelope cues."	perception
M%qxY.aq@W	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Fundamental Frequency &amp; Harmonics	"what we hear as
          a single sound or pitch when someone is speaking (for example,
          when making the sound [i]) is really a <i>fundamental
            frequency </i>Âü∫È†ª (determined by how many times the vocal
          folds vibrate in one second, and measured in cycles per second
          [cps], or hertz Ëµ´ [Hz]; named after the German physicist <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz"">Heinrich Rudolf Hertz</a>), plus a whole
          series of <i>harmonics</i> Ë´ßÈü≥ (often called Ê≥õÈü≥ on stringed instruments)
            or <i>overtones</i> ÂÄçÈü≥. These


          terms overlap in meaning, but: ""harmonic"" includes the
          fundamental frequency and all of the overtones above it, while
          ""overtones"" include all frequencies <b>greater </b><b>than</b>
          the fundamental frequency. <br>
<br>
          &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;The harmonics are <i>multiples of
            the fundamental frequency</i>. So if the fundamental
          frequency is 100 Hz, the higher harmonics will be 200 Hz, 300
          Hz, 400 Hz, 500 Hz, and so on. If the fundamental frequency
          were 220 Hz, the harmonics would be 440 Hz, 660 Hz, 880 Hz,
          and so on. In terms of intervals on the scale, we hear a base
          tone, its octave (eight notes up), then a note that is a
          twelfth up, i.e. a perfect fifth above the octave above the
          starting pitch, then a note two octaves up from the starting
          pitch, then one that is a major third above that, and on and
          on.<br><br>&nbsp;We normally don't hear the harmonics as
          separate tones, first of all because they have an increasingly
          lower amplitude than the fundamental frequency the higher up
          they go.<br><br><a href=""https://homepage.ntu.edu.tw/~karchung/phonetics%20II%20page%20eight.htm"">https://homepage.ntu.edu.tw/~karchung/phonetics II page eight.htm</a>"	perception
ugF;fPL_sH	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Missing Fundamental	"<div>A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmonic_series_(music)"">harmonic</a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sound"">sound</a> is said to have a <b>missing fundamental</b>, <b>suppressed fundamental</b>, or <b>phantom fundamental</b> when its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Overtone"">overtones</a> suggest a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_frequency"">fundamental frequency</a> but the sound lacks a component at the fundamental frequency itself.
The brain perceives the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pitch_(music)"">pitch</a> of a tone not only by its fundamental frequency, but also by the periodicity implied by the relationship between the higher <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmonic"">harmonics</a>; we may perceive the same pitch (perhaps with a different <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timbre"">timbre</a>) even if the fundamental frequency is missing from a tone.
</div><div>For example, when a note (that is not a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pure_tone"">pure tone</a>) has a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pitch_(music)"">pitch</a> of 100&nbsp;<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hertz"">Hz</a>,
 it will consist of frequency components that are integer multiples of 
that value (e.g. 100, 200, 300, 400, 500.... Hz). However, smaller 
loudspeakers may not produce low frequencies, and so in our example, the
 100&nbsp;Hz component may be missing. Nevertheless, a pitch corresponding to
 the fundamental may still be heard.&nbsp;</div><div><br></div><div><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Missing_fundamental"">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Missing_fundamental</a><br></div>"	perception

"fqmXo_U#7-"	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Tonotopy	"<div>In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physiology"">physiology</a>, <b>tonotopy</b>
 (from Greek tono = frequency and topos = place) is the spatial 
arrangement of where sounds of different frequency are processed in the 
brain. Tones close to each other in terms of frequency are represented 
in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topology"">topologically</a> neighbouring regions in the brain. Tonotopic maps are a particular case of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topographic_map_(Neuroanatomy)"">topographic</a> organization, similar to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retinotopy"">retinotopy</a> in the visual system.
</div><div>Tonotopy in the auditory system begins at the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cochlea"">cochlea</a>, the small snail-like structure in the inner ear that sends information about sound to the brain.  Different regions of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basilar_membrane"">basilar membrane</a> in the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organ_of_Corti"">organ of Corti</a>,
 the sound-sensitive portion of the cochlea, vibrate at different 
sinusoidal frequencies due to variations in thickness and width along 
the length of the membrane. Nerves that transmit information from 
different regions of the basilar membrane therefore encode frequency 
tonotopically. This tonotopy then projects through the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auditory_system#Central_auditory_system"">vestibulocochlear nerve and associated midbrain structures</a>
 to the primary auditory cortex via the auditory radiation pathway.
Throughout this radiation, organization is linear with relation to 
placement on the organ of Corti, in accordance to the best frequency 
response (that is, the frequency at which that neuron is most sensitive)
 of each neuron.</div><div><br></div><div><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tonotopy"">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tonotopy</a><br></div><div><br></div>"

 activity using functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI). While

previous functioning; at least one of the symptoms is either (1)

</div>

</div>

<div>Markedly diminished interest of pleasure in all, or almost all, activities
</div>
<div>most of the day, nearly every day.
</div>
</li>
<li>
<div>Significant unintentional weight change or appetite.
</div>

<li>

</div>

<li>

</div>

<li>

</div>

<li>

</div>
<div>every day.

<div>
<div>
<div>
<ol>
<li>
<div>1) &nbsp;Make an image made of random red dots;
</div>
</li>
<li>
<div>2) &nbsp;Duplicate the image, change these dots to cyan.
</div>
</li>
<li>
<div>3) &nbsp;Make the white transparent and superimpose the images so if you view
</div>
<div>these image with the red/cyan classes, you will see a black random dot
</div>
<div>pattern with no depth as these images are identical across the eyes*
</div>
</li>
<li>
<div>4) &nbsp;Shift some pixels within a shape <span style=""font-weight: 700;"">horizontally </span>compared to the others in
</div>
<div>one or both of these patterns
</div>
</li>
<li>
<div>5) &nbsp;Fill the gap with random pixels of the same color
</div>
</li>
<li>
<div>6) &nbsp;Superimpose the new red/cyan images and view them with red/cyan
</div>
</li>
</ol>
</div>
<div>
<div>Left eye sees this
</div>
</div>
<div>
<div>Right eye sees this
</div>
</div>

<div>glasses&nbsp;</div>

<div>Distributed vs. Modular theories</div>"	"<div>Jim
Haxby (Dartmouth): the distributed pattern of
activity across ventral temporal cortex neurons is what represents the
category, rather than different sets of neurons code different categories.</div><div>-To
test this theory Haxby
scanned people</div>
<div>in an
fMRI scanner as they viewed items from different categories&nbsp; and measured distributed responses from their
ventral temporal cortex</div><div><div></div>
<div>The
advantage of distributed representations is that they have a capacity to
represent a
large number of categories (&gt; 30,000)</div>
<div></div>
<div>This
view suggests a general machinery in ventral temporal cortex for object
categorization.</div></div><div><img src=""Screenshot 2022-12-12 at 6.08.46 PM.png"">Nancy
Kanwisher (MIT): In addition to a general-purpose machinery in ventral temporal
cortex there are specialized modules for processing ecologically-relevant
categories such as faces, bodies, words, and places.<br></div>
<div>That
is, the specific response of the face neurons enables you to perceive a face.</div><div><div>‚Ä¢To
test her theory Kanwisher scanned subjects while they viewed stimuli from
various categories and showed that there are in each subject‚Äôs brain regions in
ventral temporal cortex that respond preferentially to items of that category
over other categories</div></div><div><div>Kanwisher and colleagues
discovered in 1998 a region in the collateral sulcus (CoS) that responds more
strongly to places than other stimuli. They called it the parahippocampal place
area (PPA)</div></div><div><div>&nbsp;In 2005 a region in the occipito-temporal
sulcus (OTS) that responds more strongly to</div>
<div>&nbsp;body parts and especially limbs over other
stimuli was discovered</div></div><div><div>In 2000 Stanislas Dehaene and
colleagues discovered regions in the posterior and mid occipito-temporal
sulcus (pOTS/mOTS) that respond more strongly to words than other stimuli. They
called it the&nbsp; visual word form area,
VWFA</div></div><div><br></div><div><br></div><div><div><span style=""font-style: italic;"">Parvizi</span><span style=""font-style: italic;""> et
al J Neuroscience, 2012:&nbsp;</span>Stimulation of face-selective regions in the fusiform gyrus leads to a
specific distortion in perceiving faces. This is in line with the MODULAR theory, under which you should see facial distortion but nothing else upon stimulation</div></div>

n;qM^{EAkp	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Visual Pathways	"<div>
<div>
<div>
<div>only the left eye has access to the far periphery
of the left visual field (1) and only the right eye
has access to the far periphery of the right
visual field</div><div><br></div><div><div>
<div>
<div>
<div>At the optic chiasm, the optic
nerve carrying these representations of the
visual field partially crosses, such that the
entire left visual field is routed to the right
hemisphere of the brain and the right visual
field is routed to the left hemisphere of the
brain.&nbsp;</div>
</div>
</div></div></div><div>&nbsp;</div><div><div>
<div>
<div>
<div>The visual
representations continue to the lateral
geniculate nucleus (LGN), where they first
synapse and then continue along the optic
radiation to cortex. There, we find the second
synapse at primary visual cortex (also referred
to as V1, striate cortex or area 17).&nbsp;</div><div><br></div><div><div>
<div>
<div>
<div>Visual areas are arranged in (at least) two processing streams emerging from early
visual areas (V1, V2 and V3). One stream, referred to as the <span style=""font-weight: 700;"">dorsal stream</span>, ascends dorsally
from early visual cortex to areas along the parietal lobe. The second, <span style=""font-weight: 700;"">ventral stream</span>, on the
other hand, continues from early visual areas to the ventral aspect of the temporal lobe. These
two pathways are thought to serve different functions.</div><div><br></div><div>&nbsp;<b>Ungerleidger and Mishkin‚Äôs famous 1982 experiment. -&nbsp;</b>lesions to dorsal &amp; ventral stream with associated disruptions to ""where"" &amp; ""what"" tasks, respectively. These two pathways have been dubbed the ‚Äúwhat‚Äù (ventral) and ‚Äúwhere‚Äù (dorsal)
pathways (Ungerleider &amp; Mishkin, 1982).&nbsp;</div><div><br></div><div><div>
<div>
<div>
<div>They found that lesions in parietal cortex produced deficits in the ‚Äúwhere‚Äù task but not
the ‚Äúwhat‚Äù task, while lesions in temporal cortex produced deficits in the ‚Äúwhat‚Äù task but not the
‚Äúwhere‚Äù task. These results indicated a <span style=""font-weight: 700;"">double dissociation </span>of function across the dorsal and
ventral pathways. A double dissociation is evident if one manipulation affects the first variable
and not the second, while the other manipulation affects the second variable and not the first. In
our case, a lesion to the parietal lobe affects spatial perception but not recognition, while a
lesion to the temporal lobe affects recognition but not spatial perception.&nbsp;</div><div><br></div><div><div>
<div>
<div>
<div>In humans, damage to end stages of the ventral stream can cause deficits in object
recognition called <span style=""font-weight: 700;"">visual agnosias</span>. A visual agnosia is defined as an impairment in object
recognition that can‚Äôt be attributed to a general loss in intelligence or elementary visual
perceptual losses such as in brightness, acuity, depth or color. Check out the patient video in
module 5 (‚ÄúNOVA-stranger in the mirror‚Äù) for an example. Damage to end stages of the dorsal
stream, on the other hand, produces deficits in spatial perception and grasping. One example is
unilateral neglect, an impairment of spatial vision in which patients fail to notice things in the
visual field opposite (contralateral) to their damaged or lesioned hemisphere (check out
‚Äúneglect.mov‚Äù for an example patient). These disorders are consistent with Ungerleider and
Mishkin‚Äôs conceptualization of the ventral stream as a ‚Äúwhat‚Äù pathway and the dorsal stream as
a ‚Äúwhere‚Äù pathway.&nbsp;</div><div><br></div><div><div>
<div>
<div>
<div>However, based on a patient with a different lesion and disorder, Goodale and Milner
(1992) proposed a different function for the dorsal stream. Specifically, they proposed that the
dorsal stream is instead best characterized as a ‚Äúhow‚Äù processing stream, which is involved in
visually guided actions. Goodale and Milner also devised two tasks - a perceptual matching
task, in which the participant was instructed to indicate the orientation of card (e.g.
vertical/horizontal) and a second, visuomotor posting task, in which the participant was
instructed to rotate a card so they could post it though an oriented slot. They found that a
patient, ‚ÄúDF‚Äù, with a lesion to the ventral stream, failed the perceptual task, suggesting that she
did not have visual form perception, as predicted by Ungerleider and Mishkin‚Äôs theory. However,
DF surprisingly still performed well at the visuomotor posting task. A second patient, ‚ÄúRV‚Äù, on
the other hand, had a lesion to their dorsal stream and despite being able to perform well on the
perceptual matching task, failed the visuomotor posting task. This inability to use visual
information to reach out and grasp objects, while at the same time having no difficulty in
recognizing or describing objects in the impaired visual field is called <span style=""font-weight: 700;"">object ataxia</span>. An example
video can be seen here.
</div>
<div>Thus, Goodale and Milner also provide evidence for a double dissociation between the
ventral and dorsal streams. However, they propose a different role for the dorsal stream:
involvement in visually guided action. This is in contrast to Ungerleider and Mishkin‚Äôs proposed
role for the dorsal stream: spatial localization. The role of different visual processing streams, as
well as how many there are in the human brain, is still an active topic of research even today.&nbsp;</div>
</div>

<div></div>

tMu!%I&Rtc	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Conversational Psychology	I‚Äôm someone who has to actively listen to people for a living (and married to someone who feels compelled to frequently interrupt me for periodic, verbal affirmations).<br><br>Body language can be your best (and least disruptive) friend. Try the following things and I‚Äôm confident your friends/family/colleagues will feel you‚Äôre engaged (albeit silent).<br><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; At its most basic level, look at the person. In particular, if you slightly cock your head to the side when they‚Äôre talking, it‚Äôs one of my favorite ways of demonstrating interest and/or curiosity (if you don‚Äôt know what this looks like, picture a curious puppy who is listening to their human speak). Then, occasionally punctuate the head tilt with a few small nods (when they say something of particular note).<br><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; If you‚Äôre able to take notes while someone talks (e.g. it‚Äôs a work meeting or you‚Äôre on a phone call), that can often help tie up your brain (in a good way) while they talk. It‚Äôs also another non-disruptive way of showing interest. In contrast, if I‚Äôm sharing a ton of information with someone (particularly in a professional setting) and they‚Äôre not taking notes, I‚Äôm highly skeptical of their ability (and intent) to remember anything I‚Äôm saying.<br><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Leaning forward (e.g. in a chair) can also be a good non-verbal indication of being engaged, whereas leaning backwards (away from the speaker) can indicate the opposite.<br><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; If you start looking at your watch/phone or over their shoulder, people notice. This can actually be helpful when you want to get OUT of a conversation. Similarly, be mindful of any other body language (mannerisms) that can demonstrate impatience. For example, some people start to bounce their leg or fidget.<br><br>If you‚Äôre able to patiently wait for the person to complete their thoughts, here are some verbal responses I use all the time, which demonstrate I‚Äôm engaged and actively listening:<br><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ‚ÄúLet me see if I‚Äôm understanding correctly‚Ä¶ &lt;restate what they said in your own words&gt;‚Äù Funny enough, even if you restate 100% accurately, it often makes the other person want to talk more (expound upon things or provide more detail). Use at your own risk. :)<br><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ‚ÄúCould you tell me more about‚Ä¶ &lt;something they said&gt;?‚Äù I love this one because I can guide the conversation towards something in the conversation that I‚Äôm interested in, while simultaneously showing that I‚Äôm actively listening.<br><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ‚ÄúThank you for sharing that.‚Äù Very simple and nothing fancy, but it‚Äôs effective. Expressing some sign of gratitude for someone‚Äôs contribution to the conversation is a good way to make their contributions feel valued, especially if you have nothing further to say/ask. As an optional (but powerful) bonus, you can add in a quick note about how something they said has meaning to you (e.g. you can relate to it, it taught you something, etc.). Likewise, expressing gratitude can be a great time to end the conversation or transition the conversation to something else.	behavior sociology

The best way to take in the world is to be fascinated by, and grateful for, every shred of learning you can gain.</li>

The great purpose of an education is orientation - to grant the student as full an understanding as possible of the world and people around them, and to give them the tools and mindset to find advantage for themselves within the system.</li><li>

In any disagreement, the most effective tactic is to disarm. Focus the discussion on the other instead of yourself. Let them see their own mistakes. When necessary, take away the pleasure they may gain from making criticisms of you.</li></ol><ol>

<h2>Fundamental Techniques in handling people</h2>
<ol>
<li>Don't criticize, condemn or complain.
-People will rarely agree with you on critiques.
-Criticism always returns home.
-Positive reinforcement is always more effective - seek to uplift.
__</li>
<li>Give honest and sincere appreciation.
-The human desire to be or feel important is as deep as the desire to eat or drink.
-Flattery is counterfeit and will get you into trouble if you pass it on.
-Hurting people not only does not change them, it is never called for.
-""Be hearty in your approbation and lavish in your praise.""
__</li>
<li>""Arouse in the other person an eager want. He who can do this has the whole world with him. He who cannot walks a lonely way"" ~Harry Overstreet
-""The only way on Earth to influence other people is to talk about what they want and show them how to get it."" ~Carnegie
-""If there is any one secret of success, it lies in the ability to get the other person‚Äôs point of view and see things from that person‚Äôs angle as well as from your own.‚Äù
~Henry Ford
-Customers like to feel that they are buying, not being sold.</li>
</ol>
<h2>Six ways</h2>
<ol>
<li>Become genuinely interested in other people.
-In making friends it is much more effective to become interested in others than to try to pique their interest in you.
-""It is the individual who is not interested in his fellow men who has the greatest difficulties in life and provides the greatest injury to others. It is from among such individuals that all human failures spring."" ~Alfred Adler
-you must be passionate about people if you intend for any of your creative work to be successful with an audience, since the artist-observer relationship is somewhat reciprocative. You must like your audience for them to like your content.
__</li>
<li>Smile.
-""You must have a good time meeting people if you expect them to have a good time meeting you."" ~Carnegie
-Actions are under the control of the will, whereas emotions are not, but emotions can be controlled through actions. Hence, the only path to cheerfulness is to act as though cheer is already present.
-""Happiness doesn't depend on outward conditions. It depends on inner conditions."" ~Carnegie
-""To think rightly is to create. All things come through desire and every sincere prayer is answered...we are gods in the chrysalis."" ~Carnegie
__</li>
<li>""Remember that a person‚Äôs name is to that person the sweetest and most important sound in any language.' ~Carnegie
-Sometimes completing a mental portrait of someone by finding out a smattering of facts about them can be incredibly helpful to remembering their name.
-One way of bringing your employees closer to the company is to highlight them as individuals and show respect to their names. If someone has worked on something for you, show them that you are willing to work to comprehend the scale and detail and complexity of their work - admire the miniscule and obscure.
__</li>
<li>Be a good listener. Encourage others to talk about themselves.
-""Few human beings are proof against the implied flattery of rapt attention."" ~Jack Woodford, Strangers in Love
-""Even the most violent critic, will frequently soften and be subdued in the presence
of a patient, sympathetic listener.""
-Those who cause a scene often do so from a desire to feel important, but you can supplant that method by making them feel that importance in your conversation.
__</li>
<li>Talk in terms of the other person's interests.
-""The royal road to a person's heart is to talk about the things he or she treasures most.""
-The person will show more interest in what you have to say if you make it accessible to them.
__</li>
<li>Make the other person feel important - and do it sincerely.
-One all-important law of human conduct: Always make the other person feel important.
-The craving to be important is what separates human civilization from that of animals.
-""The unvarnished truth is that almost all the people you meet feel themselves superior to you in some way, and a sure way to their hearts is to let them realize in some subtle way that you recognize their importance, and recognize it sincerely.""</li>
</ol>
<h2>How to Win People to Your Way of Thinking</h2>
<ol><li>The only way to get the best of an argument is to avoid it.
-Nine times out of ten, an argument will only serve to deepen the conviction of each person that he is right, and so an argument is a net loss whether or not you ""win"" it.
-""A man convinced against his will is of the same opinion still.""
-If your opponent seeks to argue, cede their main point so that you may move past their argument and onto your own.
-""If you argue and rankle and contradict, you may achieve a victory sometimes; but it will be an empty victory because you will never get your opponent‚Äôs goodwill."" ~Ben Franklin
-""A misunderstanding is never ended by
an argument but by tact, diplomacy, conciliation and a sympathetic desire to see the other person‚Äôs viewpoint.""
-""Yield larger things to which you show no more than equal rights; and yield lesser ones though
clearly your own. Better give your path to a dog than be bitten by him in contesting for the right. Even killing the dog would not cure the bite.‚Äù ~Lincoln
-If you want to gain everything possible from a confrontation:
A. Welcome the disagreement.
B. Distrust your first instinctive impression.
C. Control your temper.
D. Listen first.
E. Look for areas of agreement.
F. Be honest.
G. Promise to think over your opponent's ideas and study them carefully (sense of importance).
H. Thank your opponents sincerely for their interest.
I. Postpone action to give both sides time to think through the problem.</li>
<li>Show respect for the other person's opinions. Never say, ""You're wrong.""
-Telling someone they are wrong only serves to strike a direct blow against their intelligence, characterize you as self-righteous, and put them on the defensive.
-If you are going to prove anything, do it subtly.
-""Men must be taught as if you
taught them not, and things unknown proposed as things forgot."" ~Alexander Pope
-‚ÄúYou cannot teach a man anything; you can only help him to find it within himself."" ~Galileo
-""Be wiser than other people if you can, but do not tell them so.‚Äù ~Lord Chesterfield
-‚ÄúOne thing only I know, and that is that I know
nothing.‚Äù ~Socrates
-""Nobody in the heavens above or on earth beneath or in the waters under the earth will ever object to your saying, ‚ÄúI may be wrong. Let‚Äôs examine the facts.‚Äù
-""You will never get into trouble by admitting that you may be wrong. That will stop all argument and inspire your opponent to be just as fair and open and broad-minded as you are. It will make him want to admit that he, too, may be wrong.""
-""Few people are logical.""
-""most of our so-called reasoning
consists in finding arguments for going on believing as we already do."" ~James Harvey Robinson</li><li>

If you are wrong, admit it quickly and emphatically.
-""Say about yourself all the derogatory things you know the other person is thinking or wants to say or intends to say‚Äîand say them before that person has a chance to say them.""
-Always have the courage to admit your errors, and if you sense you are about to be rebuked, beat the other person to the critical punch whenever you can.
-Self-criticism is more effective, and usually more fun, than defending yourself.
-""By fighting you never get enough, but by yielding you get more than expected.""</li><li>

Begin in a friendly way.
-""'a drop of honey catches more flies than a gallon of gall‚Äô. So with men, if you would win a man to your cause, first convince him that you are his sincere friend. Therein is a drop of honey that catches his heart; which, say what you will, is the great high road to his reason.‚Äù ~Lincoln
-Always take the high road - the friendly, sympathetic, appreciative approach. This will win people to your thinking and make them more open to your opinions and desires.</li><li>

Get the other person saying ""yes, yes"" immediately.
-A person must be started in the affirmative direction. Get them saying ""yes"" at the outset. If you begin with ""no"", all your pride of personality demands that you remain consistent with yourself.
-""Begin by emphasizing‚Äîand keep on emphasizing‚Äîthe things on which you agree. Keep emphasizing, if possible, that
you are both striving for the same end and that your only difference is one of method and not of purpose.""
-Attitude has inertia, and you create much more convincing work for yourself if you begin in the wrong direction.
-Frame necessary action as being not for your sake, but for the other person's sake. Make the other understand your desires as common between you.
-""He who treads softly goes far."" ~Chinese proverb</li><li>

Let the other person do a great deal of the talking.
-""If you want enemies, excel your friends; but if you want friends, let your friends excel you.‚Äù ~La Rochefoucauld
-""When our friends excel us, they feel important; but when we excel them, they‚Äîor at least some of them‚Äîwill feel inferior and envious.""
-If you seek friends, ask others to share their joys with you, and only tell them of yours if asked.</li><li>

Let the other person feel that the idea is his or hers.
-""No one likes to feel that he or she is being sold something or told to do a thing. We much prefer to feel that we are buying of our own accord or acting on our own ideas. We like to be consulted about our wishes, our wants, our thoughts.""
-‚ÄúIn every work of genius we recognize our
own rejected thoughts; they come back to us with a certain alienated majesty.‚Äù ~Ralph Waldo Emerson
-‚ÄúThe reason why rivers and seas receive the homage of a hundred mountain streams is that they keep below them. Thus they are able to reign over all the mountain streams. So the sage, wishing to be above men, putteth himself below them; wishing to be before them, he putteth himself behind them. Thus, though his place be above men, they do not feel his weight; though his place be before them, they do not count it an injury.‚Äù ~Lao Tse</li><li>

Try honestly to see things from the other person's point of view.
-Ask yourself how your goal can be framed by their desires.
-""I would rather walk the sidewalk in front of a person‚Äôs office for two hours before an interview than step into that office without a perfectly clear idea of what I was going to say and what that person‚Äîfrom my knowledge of his or her interests and motives‚Äîwas likely to answer."" ~Dean Donham, Harvard Business School</li><li>

Be sympathetic with the other person's ideas and desires.
-To stop arguments and cultivate in the other both good will and rapt attention, you need only to say ""I don't blame you at all for feeling the way you do. If I were in your situation I'd feel the same way.""
-""Three-fourths of the people you will ever meet are hungering and thirsting for sympathy. Give it to them, and they will love you.""
-Develop in yourself absolute sympathy for idiosyncrasies, even if they do not resemble your own, and people will recognize that you have taken the steps to understand their uniqueness.
-""Sympathy the human species universally craves."" ~Dr. Arthur I. Gates</li><li>

Appeal to the nobler motives.
-J.P. Morgan observed that people usually have two reasons for doing things: a real one and one that sounds good. Carnegie says ""the person himself will think of the real reason. You don‚Äôt need to emphasize that. But all of us, being idealists at heart, like to think of motives that sound good. So, in order to change people, appeal to the nobler motives.
-""Nothing will work in all cases‚Äîand nothing will work with all people. If you are satisfied with the results you are now getting, why change? If you are not satisfied, why not experiment?""</li><li>

Dramatize your ideas.
-""Merely stating a truth isn't enough. The truth must be made vivid, interesting, dramatic.""
-Showmanship is not a tool of deception, but can often serve to make your idea more tangible and understandable.</li><li>

Throw down a challenge.
-‚ÄúThe way to get things done is to stimulate competition. I do not mean in a sordid, money-getting way, but in the desire to excel.‚Äù ~Charles Schwab
-""That is what every successful person loves: the game. The chance for self-expression. The chance to prove his or her worth, to excel, to win.""</li></ol>

<h2>Be a Leader: How to Change People Without Giving Offense or Arousing Resentment</h2>
<h2>Before you Approach:</h2>
<ol>
<li>Be sincere. Do not promise anything that you cannot deliver. Forget about the benefits to yourself and concentrate on the benefits to the other person.</li>
<li>Know exactly what it is you want the other person to do.</li>
<li>Be empathetic. Ask yourself what is it the other person really wants.</li>
<li>Consider the benefits that person will receive from doing what you suggest.</li>
<li>Match those benefits to the other person‚Äôs wants.</li>
<li>When you make your request, put it in a form that will convey to the other person the idea that he personally will benefit.</li>
</ol>

<h2>During the Approach:</h2>
<ol>
<li>Begin with praise and honest appreciation.
-If you must criticize, the most effective way to do so is to first show the person what they have been doing well, and to appeal to their desire to do well further.
-Demonstrate that you have confidence in the person and their abilities to solve their problems, and frame their improvement as something they should want too.</li>
</ol>

<ol>
<li>Call attention to people's mistakes indirectly.
-Never use the word 'but' between praise and criticism. It will deepen the impact of your criticism by contrast, and call into question the credibility and authenticity of your praise.
-Instead, always opt for the word ""and"" and frame the critique's opposite in a positive and desirable light which supplements your praise.</li>
</ol>

<ol>
<li>Talk about your own mistakes before criticizing the other person.
-Put yourself on their level and frame their mistakes in terms of yours, and they will be much more receptive to your criticism as advice rather than attack.
-Give not just constructive criticism but also the tools and guidance with which to construct.</li>
</ol>

<ol>
<li>Always frame orders as suggestive questions.
-Give others the pride of choosing to adopt your ideas, rather than the shame of being forced to move against their will.
-A good leader illuminates the ideal path, but makes the follower believe they are choosing it.</li>
</ol>

<ol>
<li>Always let the other person save face.</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>""I have no right to say or do anything that diminishes a man in his own eyes. What matters is not what I think of him, but what he thinks of himself. Hurting a man in his dignity is a crime.‚Äù - Antoine de Saint-Exupery</li>
</ul>

<ol>
<li>Praise the slightest improvement and praise every improvement.
-""Abilities wither under criticism; they blossom under encouragement.""</li>
</ol>

<ol>
<li>Give the other person a fine reputation to live up to.
-""If you want to improve a person in a certain aspect, act as though that particular trait were
already one of his or her outstanding characteristics.""
-""Assume a virtue, if you have it not."" -Shakespeare</li>
</ol>

<ol>
<li>Use encouragement. Make the fault seem easy to correct.
-Work diligently alongside the other person to celebrate their progress and egg them on. Make the process sound easy and help make it so.</li>
</ol>

<ol>
<li>Always make the other person happy about doing the thing you suggest.
-Also display that it would make you happy - ""I would be very glad if you would...""</li></ol><div><br></div><div><br></div><div><ul>
<li><strong>Things that don‚Äôt work</strong>
<ul>
<li>Obvious, unfun or uninteresting questions - meet soon? know what to do?</li>
<li>Complimentary, Interrogatory, Quippy, Timid</li>
<li>GUESSING about someone, zeroing in on their profile, joking about their profile, asking to do something on a first date</li>
</ul>
</li></ul></div>"	economics interpersonal persuasion psychology rhetoric selfperception sociology

b<4S1i!`1_	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Pitching Ideas as Stories	"Top-down comm: <br>Thesis - ideas - data&nbsp;<div>Clean, clear, persuasive, consistent, supported<br>Working memory holds 5-9 chunks, stored quickly
<br>Chunks must be delivered in a linear order</div><div><div><h2>How to communicate clearly</h2><div><div>University teaches you to communicate the wrong way.</div><div><br></div><div>You are told that&nbsp;the best way to communicate new information is to:</div><div><br></div><ul><li>Recount all the&nbsp;context (introduction)</li><li>Describe the analyses that you've done (methodology)</li><li>Explain the&nbsp;findings&nbsp;(results)</li><li>Highlight the implications of those&nbsp;findings (discussion).</li></ul><div><br></div><div>This
 is a very bottom-up / process-oriented approach to communication. But 
it's the wrong way to communicate in a business context.</div><div><br></div><div>Why?</div><div><br></div><div>Because executives are busy, rarely care about the detail, and only care about the answer.</div><div><br></div><div><strong>So start with the answer first.</strong></div><div><br></div><div>When an executive asks them ""what should we do?"", they are expected to respond crisply with ""you should do X"".</div><div><br></div><div>And then they follow with&nbsp;a short summary of the&nbsp;3&nbsp;main arguments and the data that support them.</div><div><br></div><div><strong>So it looks something like this:</strong></div><div><br></div><ul><li>Start with&nbsp;the answer</li><li>Describe argument 1 with supporting data</li><li>Describe argument&nbsp;2&nbsp;with supporting data</li><li>Describe argument 3 with supporting data</li></ul><div><strong>So how's that sound?</strong></div><div><br></div><div>Clear. Authoritative. Persuasive.</div><div><br></div><div>The
 implication of top-down communication is that it&nbsp;forces&nbsp;you to&nbsp;have 
already synthesized and summarized the information into one simple 
recommendation and three supporting arguments.</div></div></div><br><div>##Pitching ideas as stories</div>
<ul>
<li>Make north star immediately apparent in a single line, on the first slide</li>
<li>Make the product problem clearly apparent &amp; nuanced
-explain how the solution specifically solves using an anecdote/headline
-Easier to pitch when you make the problem personal. If you begin with personal experience, show how others share it, or vice versa</li>
<li>title slide and brief intro to yourself</li>
<li>""We're solving [problem] for [audience] with [company/product].""</li>
<li>segue into a demo and prove thoroughly through a user journey (2mins max) how the problem is clearly solved, and resonant for that user</li>
<li>Hit them with data, details, partnerships, flash, trends, opportunities, narratives from their space, business acumen, concrete analysis
-How are YOU the investment as well as your product?
-Paint a happily ever after, connect the north star, showing how you would use funding and where you're going next.</li>
<li>Finally, the ASK slide should be straightforward with expected success/failure metrics, requirements, milestones</li>
<li>Clinch with 5-6 most concrete/persuasive investor benefits</li>
<li>If you get a moment, pitch your story and why you personally sought out this solution</li><li>If it doesn‚Äôt contribute meaning, it‚Äôs waste - Henry Ford</li><li>Ask a test group to read your pitch and pitch it back. They should be able to understand with no context</li><li>GPT is a subpar substitute for this function</li><li>As a killer appendix for subtle evidence of intelligence, research, investigated objections, help alleviate stress about cutting deck material</li></ul><div><br></div><div>NPR - What makes a good Pitch?</div><div><ol><li><b>an emergent and clear narrative</b>&nbsp;- an event, a topic, or a planned trip isn't good enough. Narrative elements: focus, central question, stakes, conflict, a protagonist</li><li><b>demonstrates reporting</b>&nbsp;- illustrates that the reporter has already made some calls, gathered info, ID'd main players Editors want proof it's not a wild goose chase Why important? Why now? How now?</li><li><b>is short</b>&nbsp;- good intentions but little time - play to that Science editor Chris Joyce: ‚ÄúIf a reporter can‚Äôt pitch a story in one or, at most, two sentences, he or she doesn‚Äôt know what the story is.‚Äù first few and last few lines are most important</li><li><b>isn't in a vacuum</b>&nbsp;- fit into a crowded stream of similar coverage while differentiating yourself from stories that have already been told</li><li><b>considers the audience</b>&nbsp;- why should they care as much as you do?</li><li><b>Is surprising&nbsp;</b>- the best pitches make editors curious to read, discover, and ask more questions. q3,]Y&*CQI	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Child Development - Sensitive Period	"Montessori theory - moment when a child's inclination/focus toward a certain kind of learning is heightened<br><br>""During their first six years of life, children move through five main 
categories of sensitive periods, including: order, language, sensory 
skills, movement, and social skills. Each sensitive period lasts for as 
long as it is necessary for a child to complete a particular stage in 
their development. These periods of special sensitivity are only 
temporary and fade once the aim is accomplished.<br><br>Other characteristics of sensitive periods may include mimicking, 
intense concentration, and compulsive or obsessive behaviours. 
Interrupting a child while they are in the middle of an intense 
sensitive period can result in a powerful emotional response such as a 
tantrum. Break a routine that a child is attempting to understand and 
master, such as getting dressed, bath time or bedtime, and some children
 will emotionally fall apart.""<br><br><ol><li><b>Order -&nbsp;</b></li><ol><li>The sensitive period for order begins at birth, peaks in the second year
 of development, and continues through to around age five.During this period of sensitivity, children may be more interested in 
putting things in order and packing things away, than they are in 
playing with their toys.&nbsp;</li><li>To support the sensitive period for order, it 
is important to establish ground rules, a solid routine, and create an 
organised environment where everything has a place.</li></ol><li><b>Language -&nbsp;</b></li><ol><li>From birth to age six, children are in the sensitive period for 
language. Sensitivity to language involves three key phases: spoken 
language, written language, and reading. The sensitive period for spoken
 language is from 7 months to 3 years of age. It begins when the child 
first creates sounds by mimicking mouth movements, and progresses over 
time, as they learn to form words and simple sentences.</li><li>The sensitive period for learning to write is from 3.5 to 4.5 years 
of age. This begins when the child learns the alphabet, and then sight 
words, which form the foundation for reading and writing skills.</li><li>For reading, a child is intensely interested from 4.5 to 5.5 years of
 age. Reading skills are often developed after a child learns to write 
as it involves visual tracking skills.</li></ol><li><b>Sensory Skills</b>&nbsp;-&nbsp;</li><ol><li>The first phase of sensory awareness, sensitivity to small objects, 
occurs from one to three years of age. This period can be characterised 
by a child‚Äôs fixation with small objects and tiny details.</li><li>The second sensitivity phase, sensorial exploration and classification, 
occurs from 2.5 to 6 years. This phase can be classified by an intense 
desire to take part in learning experiences that integrate the senses.</li></ol><li><b>Movement</b>&nbsp;-&nbsp;</li><ol><li>From birth to 2.5 years, children are sensitive to gross and fine motor 
development. This begins when the infant child learns to crawl, pull up,
 and eventually walk without assistance.</li><li>From 2.5 to 4.5 years of age, children enter the sensitive period for 
refinement and coordination of movement. This is when the child begins 
to hold items using both hands, develop the pincer grip, and control and
 coordinate movement.</li></ol><li><b>Social Skills</b>&nbsp;-&nbsp;</li><ol><li>From 2.5 to 5 years, children learn that they are part of a group and develop an intense interest in social relationships.</li><li>To support the development of social skills in your child, it is 
important to provide your child with opportunities to socialise with 
children their own age i.e. at playgroup or childcare. At home, you can 
model positive social behaviours, and practice grace and courtesy 
rituals such as saying ‚Äúplease‚Äù and ‚Äúthank you.‚Äù</li></ol></ol>

"CCsk#UP@t#"	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	The Friedman Doctrine	"<div>Milton Friedman, 1970</div><div>A business's chief social responsibility is to increase its profits, because the social responsibility of the business to its customers is to increase its profits.</div>
<div>Businessmen who preach that businesses should have a ""social conscience"" are preaching ""pure and unadulterated socialism"". Defending philanthropy as a cornerstone of free enterprise makes you an unwitting puppets controlled by intellectual forces which are undermining free society.</div>
<div>It's difficult to assess how well a leader leads, but indisputable that they contractually agreed to do exactly that.</div>
<div>If an individual believes in giving to organizations, those are the social responsibilities of the person, not their company. If social responsibility is not pure rhetoric, we understand that socially responsible companies must by definition do something against the interests of their shareholders. Committing to socially responsible causes means taking money from employers, employees, or customers - which is against the interests of the company. It's essentially taxation without representation.</div>
<div>Public service nullifies your shareholder obligations &amp; demonstrates your priority is being a public servant. If you're a CEO making this decision, you must have good political machinery in place to manage a large transfer. If you're a shareholder, even more so. This is why union leaders object to government market interference much more than business leaders. Sole proprietors have more freedom &amp; less impact than groups which operate as a collective.</div>
<h2>Social responsibility on a company level harms free society by using justice as an aegis for corporate embezzlement.</h2>
<div>Corporate social responsibility preaches non-collectivist means for collectivist ends. However, the most collectivist a company can be is simply to engage in free and open competition without coercion, fraud, or deception, and to attempt to secure the benefits of cooperative business.</div>"	economics history sociology

O1g?AY=nH}	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Impress Managers by...	"<div><div><strong>1. Remind them where you left off</strong></div><div><br></div><div>You've been working on one specific task for quite a while now -- and you want to update your manager.</div><div><br></div><div>Unlike you, your manager is switching between tasks all day.</div><div>In fact, they are probably reading your email or message in the 2-minute space they have between meetings.</div><div><br></div><div>You
 should assume that they are thinking about other things. And you should
 remind them where you left off, so they can switch tasks faster.</div><div><br></div><div>‚õîÔ∏è&nbsp;<strong>Don't do this:</strong></div><div><br></div><div>""Here's the updated doc.""</div><div><br></div><div>‚úÖ&nbsp;<strong>Do this:</strong></div><div><br></div><div>""Here's
 the updated steering committee presentation. I've updated the document 
with the updated financial forecast. Let me know if you're happy with 
this version and I'll distribute to participants tonight.""</div><div><br></div><div><strong>2. Make sure your ""ask"" is clear</strong></div><div><br></div><div>Your boss isn't a mind reader.</div><div><br></div><div>Even if they are across the detail of your work, you shouldn't assume that they know what you need from them.</div><div><br></div><div>Most managers assume that, unless you tell them otherwise, things are progressing as planned.</div><div><br></div><div>Don't surprise them by telling them you've been blocked for days.</div><div>You should be specific and explicit about what you need from them. And ensure that you ask them early.</div><div><br></div><div>‚õîÔ∏è&nbsp;<strong>Don't do this:</strong></div><div><br></div><div>""I've
 updated the financial model with most of the new assumptions. But still
 waiting on sales assumptions from FP&amp;A before I can finalize the 
model.""</div><div><br></div><div>‚úÖ&nbsp;<strong>Do this:</strong></div><div><br></div><div>""I've
 updated the financial model with most of the new assumptions. However, 
I've been waiting for the sales assumptions from FP&amp;A for 5 days, 
and they're no longer replying to my emails. Can you please email James 
today and CC me to help get this unblocked?""</div><div><br></div><div><strong>3. Make sure your ""non-ask"" is clear</strong></div><div><br></div><div>You shouldn't just be emailing your manager when there's an ask. You should also be sharing regular status updates.</div><div><br></div><div>And it's also very important that your manager knows that these emails are just an FYI.</div><div><br></div><div>Don't make your manager guess.</div><div><br></div><div>If you're sending an update as an FYI, you should explicitly tell them that no action is required.</div><div><br></div><div>‚õîÔ∏è&nbsp;<strong>Don't do this:</strong></div><div><br></div><div>""I've
 updated the financial model with most of the new assumptions. But still
 waiting on sales assumptions from FP&amp;A before I can finalize the 
model.""</div><div><br></div><div>‚úÖ&nbsp;<strong>Do this:</strong></div><div><br></div><div>""I've
 updated the financial model with most of the new assumptions. But still
 waiting on sales assumptions from FP&amp;A before I can finalize the 
model. I'll give them another day and then let you know if I need you to
 help follow up to unblock this, so no action right now.""</div><div><br></div><div><strong>4. Demonstrate a bias to action</strong></div><div><br></div><div>As a manager, there's nothing worse than having to hand-hold somebody to complete a task.</div><div><br></div><div>Don't
 get me wrong, a good manager should unblock their team member if they 
are stuck. But the team member should have tried to work independently 
first.</div><div><br></div><div>The best way to show that you're independent is to show a bias to action.</div><div><br></div><div>You
 should always be trying to achieve the task, even if it requires trying
 a number of different approaches. Communicate this with your manager.</div><div><br></div><div>‚õîÔ∏è&nbsp;<strong>Don't do this:</strong></div><div><br></div><div>""I
 couldn't start writing the memo because the distribution team was too 
busy to share the audited view of this year's sales data with me.""</div><div><br></div><div>‚úÖ&nbsp;<strong>Do this:</strong></div><div><br></div><div>""I've
 finished a first draft of the memo. The distribution team hasn't had a 
chance to share the audited view of this year's sales data, so I've used
 unaudited data as a placeholder. They should be directionally correct 
and I'll update the numbers as soon as I get the audited version.""</div><div><br></div><div><strong>5. Communicate your thought process</strong></div><div><br></div><div>Because managers aren't doing tasks themselves, they have a constant underlying concern that the work or decision isn't correct.</div><div><br></div><div>And
 as a team member, the best way to give your manager reassurance that 
you've done the right thing is to share your criteria and assumptions.</div><div><br></div><div>Best case scenario is you impress your manager with your rigor.</div><div><br></div><div>Worst case scenario is they help you address any gaps in your thinking.</div><div><br></div><div>When sharing your work or communicating a decision, always share your criteria and assumptions.</div><div><br></div><div>‚õîÔ∏è&nbsp;<strong>Don't do this:</strong></div><div><br></div><div>""We should outsource our technical development to an agency.""</div><div><br></div><div>‚úÖ&nbsp;<strong>Do this:</strong></div><div><br></div><div>""We
 should outsource our technical development to an agency for three 
reasons: we'll get to market faster, they'll use best practices, and we 
can scale up/down quickly. The risk is that we lose context and 
knowledge of any technical decisions when they leave, but we'll assign 
one of our staff to do a comprehensive handover.""</div><div><br></div></div>"	career interpersonal persuasion
o}5=E<Kig&	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	üí™Strength Training; Routines; Progression	"<div>Progressive overload is the name of the game. Every day, you are trying to exceed your past self.</div><div>Exercise - sets; rest time; goal for each day</div><div><ul><li>Repeat until <b>2-3 reps AWAY FROM failure</b>; don't want overtraining issues or form breakdown, which leads to joint damage.</li><li>Between sets <b>rest 45-60sec</b>, as needed.</li><li>Do ALL exercises to prevent posture/form issues by working counteracting muscle groups.</li><li>Fully rest for at least 24hours each week. This reduces injury risk and muscle fatigue, and improves performance, as well as supporting healthy sleep by letting your exercise hormones (cortisol and adrenaline) return to baseline.</li></ul></div><ol><li>Push-ups - 4s; aim &gt;100</li><ol><li>Engage pectorals, deltoids, triceps, trapezius, rotator cuffs</li><li>Helpful for rotator cuff recovery; increase control by holding at bottom for 1sec</li><li>Progression: knee push-ups, pauses between push-ups, planks, standard, feet elevated, wider/diamond, medicine ball, holding at max effort before release</li><li>Form:&nbsp;</li><li>keep arms at 45 degree angle,&nbsp;</li><li>lower as close to ground as possible without touching, raise PAST full extension to engage rotator cuffs and shoulders fully.&nbsp;</li><li>By default you&nbsp;<span style=""font-family: -apple-system; font-size: 16px;"">place hands 2 inches wider than shoulders, but v</span>arying hand width is fine. keep your wrist in a near-vertical line with your shoulder when viewed from the side.&nbsp;</li><li>Take note of shoulder protraction and depression, or pulling your shoulders back and down into stable stacking before starting. Spine straightening muscles locked: glutes, quads, abs, rhomboids/lats locked.</li></ol><li>Pull-ups - 4s; aim &gt;50</li><ol><li>Engage latissmus dorsi, rhomboids, posterior deltoids, biceps, rotator cuffs</li><li>Helpful for rotator cuff recovery; increase control by holding at top for 1sec</li><li>Progression: assisted pull-ups (band or machine), Pull-up negatives, Pike pull-ups, neutral grip</li><li>Form: engage back muscles; keep hands a couple inches beyond shoulder width, pause at top</li></ol><li>Squats - 4s; aim &gt;100</li><ol><li>Engage quadriceps, glutes, hamstrings, core</li><li>Progression: Wall sits, Chair squats</li><li>Form:&nbsp;</li><li>weight on heels, feet hip-width apart, toes pointed slightly outward, knees over but never beyond toes</li><li>torso upright with square shoulders, glutes and core engaged throughout. Keep your chin up and your spine straight.&nbsp;</li><li>Aim to bottom out SLIGHTLY below where your thighs are parallel with the ground.</li></ol><li>High-intensity cardio - aim &gt;30min</li><ol><li>I jump rope or run</li><li>For major health benefits, the <a href=""https://www.niddk.nih.gov/health-information/weight-management/myths-nutrition-physical-activity?dkrd=hispt0934"">National Institutes of Health</a>&nbsp;(<a href=""https://www.niddk.nih.gov/health-information/weight-management/myths-nutrition-physical-activity?dkrd=hispt0934"">https://www.niddk.nih.gov/health-information/weight-management/myths-nutrition-physical-activity?dkrd=hispt0934</a>)&nbsp; recommends doing at least 150 minutes of moderate-intensity 
cardiovascular activity, or 75 minutes of high-intensity activity, each 
week. So hit the treadmill, take those stairs or start jogging.<br></li></ol><li>Uneven surfaces: step-up to knee, jumps, tricep dips,&nbsp;</li></ol><div>Generalized form notes:</div><div><ul><li><b>Squeeze spine-stabilizing muscles</b> for all exercises. This means core, rhomboids, glutes. Keep spine straight.</li><li>C<b>ontrol over power</b>. Adding time under tension builds strength as easily as adding weight.</li><li>You NEED a rest day when:</li><ul><li>Persistently sore; extremely fatigued; experiencing joint pain; emotionally imbalanced; Sleepless/stressed; Underperforming</li></ul><li>The Crossfit workout ""Angie"" defines much more than what I'd accept:</li><ul><li>100 pull-ups, 100 push-ups, 100 sit-ups, 100 squats</li><li><a href=""https://www.mensjournal.com/health-fitness/crossfit-wod-angie"">https://www.mensjournal.com/health-fitness/crossfit-wod-angie</a><br></li></ul></ul></div><div><br></div><div><br></div><div>Work from smaller sets to larger sets.
