u*:|V5T6gg	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Case Interview Secrets	"<div>Case interview secrets</div>
<ul>
<li>Out of 400 Stanford applicants, McKinsey accepted 6, a 1.5% admission rate</li>
<li>Seven parts: process overview, quantitative assessments, real-world fundamentals, problem-solving frameworks, candidate-led interview, other formats, pulling skills together</li>
<li>Seven types of evaluation formats in two categories:</li>
<ul>
<li>Hypothetical-situation case interview</li>
<ul>
<li>Candidate-led case interview:&nbsp;</li>
<ul>
<li>extremely general starting prompt e.g. should we enter XY market; how do we stop losing money</li>
<li>Silence from interviewer for rest of interview</li>
<li>YOU decide what questions to ask</li>
</ul>
<li>Interviewer-led case interview (McKinsey exclusively uses)</li>
<ul>
<li>Interviewer decides what‚Äôs important, asks you specific questions</li>
<li>Lots of jumping around in topic, which can be jarring</li>
</ul>
<li>Written case interview</li>
<ul>
<li>Somewhere between 5 and 40 charts, with an hour or two to review all information</li>
<li>Written test administered after this time</li>
</ul>
<li>Group case interview - typically a 4-person group will get several exhibits and an open ended question. You score points by helping colleagues, not sabotage</li>
<li>Presentation-only interview - overlap with written, often paired, lots of exhibits to choose from, pure polish gets you the position</li>
</ul>
<li>Quantitative assessments</li>
<ul>
<li>Many include a mini-case</li>
<li>The quantitative test- McKinsey PST</li>
<ul>
<li>No business knowledge required</li>
<li>Read questions literally and carefully; only answer the question asked, with the data presented</li>
<li>for math - Arithmetic, fractions, percentage calculations</li>
<li>data interpretation - chart analysis &amp; which of four conclusions is NOT supported</li>
<li>numerical critical reasoning - based on the date, which strategy is advantageous?</li>
<li>Take sample tests; practice fundamental skills e.g. math word problems</li>
<li>Find GRE question sets</li>
<li>The above is 70% of the PST, quantitative speed and accuracy, large number estimation</li>
</ul>
<li>The estimation question - use assumptions to solve pen-and-paper math problems</li>
<ul>
<li>Typically, estimate the size of a particular market</li>
<ul>
<li>How many gallons does a gas station pump each week?</li>
<li>How long to relocate an average mountain with an average dump truck?</li>
</ul>
<li>Make a few assumptions where needed, and do the math</li>
<li>Assess HOW you solve problems rather than how accurately</li>
<li>Common format: determine whether an opportunity is worth considering by evaluating whether estimated financial impact is even remotely close to minimum expected financial return. This can eliminate 80% of opportunities</li>
<li>Assess best and worst case scenario customer impact, product price impact, revenue impact</li>
<li>You are hired to end three-year debates between executives in ten minutes of calculation</li>
<ul>
<li>Clients value a consultant‚Äôs ability to resolve debates of opinion using estimates based on reasonable assumptions</li>
<li>Consulting firms value people with stellar client service</li>
</ul>
<li>Skill #1: precise arithmetic with large numbers</li>
<ul>
<li>Market size * market share * revenue per buyer</li>
<li>Order of operations: big even numbers first, split complex problems into multiple simple problems, do percentages and fractions last</li>
<li>‚ÄúA long series of simple math problems rather than a short series of complicated ones‚Äù</li>
<li>Arrange before you ever compute</li>
</ul>
<li>Skill 2: rounding numbers intelligently</li>
<ul>
<li>Sometimes quick, directionally correct answers are much better than slow precise ones</li>
<li>Remember whether estimation makes your final answer low or high, and counterweight for that in results and subsequent calculations</li>
<li>Alternate rounding up and rounding down, use physical hand signals to help you remember weighting bias discreetly if needed</li>
<li>Most consultants typically consider +-20% margin of error acceptable on large estimation</li>
</ul>
<li>Skill #3: finding a proxy</li>
<ul>
<li>Substitute product whose market behavior is similar to a product in question</li>
<li>Population size is usually a good proxy for market size, but not always</li>
<li>How many liters of gas does a filling station pump per week?</li>
<ul>
<li>Number of pumps a station has</li>
<li>Number of cars that drive by</li>
<li>Average percentage of pumps in use</li>
<li>Average volume of gas pumped per car</li>
</ul>
<li>It‚Äôs 1980 and Motorola has just released a failing product called the cellular phone. As costs and prices decline, what will sales be in 1985?&nbsp;</li>
<ul>
<li>Proxy is other young tech markets like fax machines, microwaves, TVs on their first three years</li>
<li>How much did a price drop of 30% affect these technologies once they had broken out of their torturous and expensive first few years?</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li>Skill #4: identifying how your proxy is imperfect</li>
<ul>
<li>Identify if something is closer to an upper limit than a precise estimate</li>
<li>In the gas pump example, know that customers will be highest during rush hours and calculate for those rush hours rather than for 24/7 pumping</li>
<li>Rule out certain proxies based on their correlation to the desired value</li>
</ul>
<li>Skill #5: segment estimates to minimize proxy imperfections</li>
<ul>
<li>Split a vague estimate into multiple precise estimates: the rate at which a gas pump is used should be split into peak, average, and trough hours. The total gallons pumped on a weekday is the sum of these things</li>
</ul>
<li>Skill #6: solving sub-estimates via assumptions, or guesstimating&nbsp;</li>
<ul>
<li>Peak hours gallons pumped = # pumps * # % pumps utilized * # cars filled per hour * # gallons per car</li>
<li>Guess that a station has 8 pumps,&nbsp;</li>
<li>Guess 80% in use at peak hours</li>
<li>Guess 6-minutes fill, I.e. a pump can fill ten cars per hour</li>
<li>Guess 10gal gas tanks because many are not completely empty when filled</li>
<li>So find gallons per hour and multiply by number of peak hours</li>
<li>Use peak hours as a proxy for off-peak hours by halving cars present, then multiplying that halved gallons-per-hour by the number of off-peak hours</li>
</ul>
</ul>
</ul>
</ul>
<li>Why case interviews exist</li>
<ul>
<li>What a firm does changes yearly &amp; often changes from one interviewer to another</li>
<li>The interview stimulates the job, designed to find good consultants. If you hate the interview, you‚Äôll hate the job</li>
<li>You answer more estimation questions as a consultant than as a candidate</li>
<li>All yourself what you would feel comfortable delivering to a client when your firm‚Äôs reputation is on the line</li>
<li>The first stage of proving value is most important for a client, as they try to justify the extra expense for relatively less experienced advisors</li>
<ul>
<li>Ask thought-provoking, insightful, unconsidered questions</li>
<li>Analyze data to discover unforeseen, counterintuitive insights that lead to new decisions</li>
</ul>
<li>Often clients will actively look to discredit you, so avoid the two cardinal sins:</li>
<ul>
<li>Offending a client representative at any level by being rude, arrogant or dismissive</li>
<li>Stating a conclusion you can‚Äôt support with data</li>
</ul>
<li>Need analytic interpersonal intelligence</li>
<ul>
<li>Clients interpret nervousness as a lack of conviction in your proposed solution, which is why answering a case perfectly but nervously will get you rejected</li>
<li>You‚Äôre often getting graded on how the message is delivered, not the content of the message itself</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li>What interviewers look for and why</li>
<ul>
<li>Interviewer look for candidates who seem like colleagues already; act your wage</li>
<li>Sell the interviewer on you; switch the tone from evaluative to collaborative</li>
<li>Consultants and clientele are constantly under tension about who‚Äôs screwing who</li>
<li>Team size and constraints are determined by the client, not the consultancy firm</li>
<li>New clients often instill a huge pressure to overdeliver</li>
<li>Each client project has its own profit &amp; loss statement, so each consultant has two billing rates: one hourly/daily rate for the consultants contribution to the client, and one for their cost to the firm, e.g. salary, rent, electricity etc</li>
<ul>
<li>These rates are rarely published, but sent to track whether a client engagement is profitable for the firm</li>
<li>Goal is always high-quality work at low cost &amp; maximum profit</li>
<li>Onboarding for new hires often offsets their value it turns them negative</li>
<li>Firms force engagement teams to take on first-year consultants by waiving that persons ‚Äúcost‚Äù to the team P&amp;L</li>
</ul>
<li>Value of an independent problem-solver&nbsp;</li>
<ul>
<li>Extreme tests are often used to gauge new hires‚Äô aptitude for independence and problem-solving</li>
<li>Author was asked to generate $200m new profit in 3-5 years, no other guidance</li>
<li>You need to solve the problem competently and make the firm look good</li>
<li>‚ÄúWill my current work get easier or harder if you‚Äôre on my team?‚Äù</li>
<li>Independent problem solvers hiring more of the same</li>
</ul>
<li>‚ÄúWhat KEY info is needed to answer the client‚Äôs question?‚Äù</li>
<ul>
<li>Easy weed out question, ends many interviews</li>
<li>Clients mainly ask yes/no or ‚Äúwhat should we do?‚Äù Questions, eg</li>
<ul>
<li>Should we merge/close division/enter market segment</li>
<li>There‚Äôs XY conflict emerging now. What do we do?</li>
<li>Often selling directional yes/no answers, even with vague questions</li>
</ul>
<li>They often want a fast and simple answer to a complex question, not a complex and precise answer</li>
<ul>
<li>This is why interviewers screen out candidates who can‚Äôt get comfortable with being ‚Äúaccurate enough‚Äù - ‚Äúprecisely accurate‚Äù is a negative</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li>Often right but never without factual justification</li>
<ul>
<li>Sell confidence in not knowing the right answer, if you have to. Sell confidence in suspecting something true if you have to. Caveat your skepticism and sell confidence in your skepticism.</li>
<li>‚ÄúI don‚Äôt have the facts to be 100% certain‚Äù</li>
<li>You speak your firm‚Äôs POV; sell it well</li>
<li>A client only hears your firm‚Äôs opinion, not yours&nbsp;</li>
<li>The biggest nightmare is that a new hire will embarrass the firm</li>
<li>Interviewers nitpick because clients do</li>
<li>Be prepared to defend every possible situation to substantiate a course of action. You must demonstrate that your advice will ALWAYS hold, no matter what issues the interviewer/client brings up</li>
<li>2 ways to get too often right but never without justification:</li>
<ul>
<li>Gather more factual evidence for your argument</li>
<li>Soften your arguments language to leverage already gathered knowledge</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li>Being correct diplomatically and client-friendly</li>
<ul>
<li>Clients do not accept factually accurate recommendations, they accept factually supported recommendations they can UNDERSTAND</li>
<li>communication just as important as problem solving</li>
<li>Think and communicate linearly, and draw through lines from A to C at the end of your argument</li>
<li>‚ÄúThe right answer is pointless unless the client can understand the process undertaken to get there.‚Äù</li>
<li>Senior clients may not even tell you they‚Äôre confused until your proposal gets fully struck down because they want to save face</li>
<li>Communication skills separate the final interview rounds from the job offer</li>
</ul>
<li>The airplane test, or don‚Äôt be a dick&nbsp;</li>
<ul>
<li>You‚Äôre an outsider by default - sometimes an unwanted, distrusted one. Tough clients hope you will prove them right that you‚Äôre terrible</li>
<li>Adapt to your clients‚Äô speed of life and work</li>
<li>Your relationship with your recruiter is your relationship with a client</li>
</ul>
<li><b>Process excellence </b>- when a candidate can consistently follow a problem-solving process. You satisfy both needs:</li>
<ul>
<li>You can follow a structured, analytical process</li>
<li>You can reach the correct conclusions from that process</li>
<li>The first point here is much more important- consistent protocol over correctness in allotted time</li>
<li>Multiple case interviews weed out candidates who get lucky in their accuracy and seeks process-oriented hires</li>
<li>Incomplete answers from excellent processes are permissible; you will eventually ALWAYS get the right answer</li>
<li>Easier to coach someone to solve problems faster than coach them to be more consistently accurate</li>
<li>Interviewer considers what it will be like to manage you</li>
</ul>
<li>CEO-level communication skills</li>
<ul>
<li>Optimize your advice from a high-level executive perspective and don‚Äôt waste clients‚Äô time with employee-level details</li>
<li>However, understanding employee-level details is essential to understanding what‚Äôs wrong with a company</li>
<li>Integrate micro-recommendations into a big-picture strategy and support those recommendations with factual analysis, instilling confidence in the client</li>
<li>CLARITY and CONFIDENCE make corporate action much more likely, and advice much more valuable</li>
<li>SYNTHESIS is central to recommendation because it provides context: do X because it will have Y effect on Z. Connect the dots and ramifications, and synthesize what will happen if the client follows, or ignores, the recommendations</li>
<li>Synthesis also entails high polish and appropriate detail</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li><b>Core problem-solving tools - scientific method/ iterative cycle</b></li>
<ul>
<li><b>Hypothesis</b></li>
<ul>
<li>Give instant hypothesis before you start to analyze, then revise that hypothesis as you discover more</li>
<li>If profit is down XY amount, examine profit components ie sales, expenses and costs.&nbsp;</li>
<li>Intended to reduce the range of possible conclusions, not widen the variety</li>
<li>Also intended to allow you to reach SOME conclusion with half the time you need to reach THE conclusion</li>
<li>Introduce between 30 seconds and 5 minutes into case work, asking background details as necessary</li>
<li>Never forget your hypothesis in the first portion - two out of three get caught up clarifying. If you absolutely need more than 5 minutes to hypothesize, state that</li>
<li>A late hypothesis WILL get you rejected; an early hypothesis MIGHT</li>
<li><b>The hypothesis is more important than the framework. The case interview is hypothesis-driven, not framework-driven. Critical thinking over formula recall.</b></li>
</ul>
<li><b>Issue tree/logical framework</b></li>
<ul>
<li>A consultant‚Äôs substitute for an experiment</li>
<li>Sets out logical conditions with logical sub-conditions under which the conclusion is certainly correct</li>
<li>Interviewers want to see your ENTIRE problem-solving strategy to front. Cover your main branches, and then begin drill down to structure the logic of each branch</li>
<li>For the common issue of declining profits, the profitability framework divides into two trees:</li>
<ul>
<li>Profit = sales revenue - operating costs</li>
<ul>
<li>Revenue = Price per unit * units sold</li>
<li>Cost = units sold * cost/unit</li>
<ul>
<li>Cost/unit = fixed c/u + variable c/u</li>
</ul>
</ul>
</ul>
<li>Every issue has an incentive/benefit framework for all involved parties</li>
<li>Weak/strong problem structuring grades are essential to the offer</li>
<ul>
<li>State issue tree early and logically</li>
<li>Don‚Äôt omit key conditions</li>
</ul>
<li><b>VALIDITY TESTING</b></li>
<ul>
<li><b>Top 2 interviewer complaints:</b> mechanical formula-based approaches lacking critical thinking; lacking a hypothesis</li>
<li><b>Hypothesis</b></li>
<ul>
<li>Issue tree formation requires, and is predicated on, hypothesi<b>s</b></li>
</ul>
<li><b>McKinsey MECE test</b></li>
<ul>
<li>Mutually exclusive, collectively exhaustive</li>
<li>Categorizes decision-making factors and grades your ability to group information into discrete mutually exclusive categories which cover all possible options (collectively exhaustive)</li>
<li>Discrete categories with no overlap between, which together cover all possible factors</li>
<li>E.g. profits = revenue-cost</li>
<ul>
<li>Cost = fixed cost + total variable cost</li>
<li>TVC = cost/unit * # units manufactured</li>
</ul>
<li>For profit to increase, cost must decrease our revenue must increase. Nothing is overlapped, nothing is left out, all things are presented in categories. MECE approved</li>
<li>Financial vs non-financial factors also work</li>
<li>Internal vs external factors also work</li>
<li>Hypothesis: introducing xyz product makes sense.</li>
<ul>
<li>Favorable customer factors</li>
<li>Favorable competitive environment</li>
<li>Favorable company operations factors</li>
<li>Favorable product-inherent aspects</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li><b>Victor Cheng ‚Äúconclusiveness‚Äù test</b></li>
<ul>
<li>Ensure using an issue tree that if all branches turn out to be true, the hypothesis must be true</li>
<li>Every branch must be factually backed and factually fallible; each branch must itself have high conclusiveness</li>
<li>Hypothesis: Apple should introduce the iPad.</li>
<ul>
<li>Customer - future market size <b>huge</b></li>
<li><b>Clients</b></li>
<li>Competition - competitive <b>vacuum</b></li>
<li><b>Products</b></li>
<li>Company - unique product design <b>advantage</b></li>
<li>ROI - potential financial <b>benefit far outweighs</b> R&amp;D costs</li>
</ul>
</ul>
</ul>
</ul>
<li> <b>Drill-down analysis</b></li>
<ul>
<li>Create logical dead ends either by proving or disproving branches of the tree, and showing those argument elements valid or invalid</li>
<li>You are spending 75% of time on PROBLEM ISOLATION AND DEFINITION, and 25% on PROBLEM SOLVING</li>
<ul>
<li>Never propose a solution until you‚Äôve isolated/defined problem</li>
<li>The solution is just a formality if you‚Äôve defined correctly</li>
<li>If you reach the end of the case without an obvious answer:</li>
<ul>
<li>You set up an incorrect issue tree.</li>
<li>You missed the full depth of a branch or eliminated incorrectly.</li>
<li>The interviewer gave you a key piece of info which you glossed over without analysis, and missed the ability to eliminate a branch.</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li>Process of logical elimination by order of relevance, validity, and soundness under the hypothesis</li>
<li>Revise hypothesis instantly if you discover inconsistency; this is why this stage exists.&nbsp;</li>
<li><b>State EVERYTHING aloud in your thought process: ‚Äú</b>My hypothesis is that the profitability issue is caused by a revenue decline, not a cost increase. I‚Äôd like to start by analyzing the revenue side and circle back to the cost side if necessary‚Äù.<b> </b>‚ÄúHmm, this new evidence casts some doubt on my original hypothesis.‚Äù</li>
<li>Not stating logical conflicts = Not understanding conflicts in the interviewers eyes&nbsp;</li>
<li><b>Start with the most uncertain branch, or the most central to your hypothesis which could most easily prove/disprove it.</b></li>
<li><b>Use a 70-30 ratio of quantitative-to-qualitative analysis.</b></li>
<ul>
<li>Quantify EVERY branch by asking for pricing, unit sales, cost data going back several years. Ask for competitor data as well. Verify any qualitative claims made.</li>
<li>If costs have increased AND revenue has declined, measure percentage of profit decline attributable to each. You may uncover a 75-25 split between revenue and cost issues.&nbsp;</li>
<li>Quantitative helps answer WHAT is causing a business symptom and by HOW MUCH.</li>
<li>qualitative helps determine WHY a certain decision was made by a client, and HOW process decisions are made.</li>
<li>YOU‚ÄôRE ALLOWED TO ASK BASIC WHY QUESTIONS</li>
<ul>
<li>What percentage of profit decline came from a drop in sales?</li>
<li>Why have transportation costs gone up for the client but not for the rest of the industry? How is the transportation process different for the client, and why?</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li><b>Don‚Äôt stop branch analysis until you reach a sub-conclusion</b></li>
<ul>
<li>Stopping point determined by 70% hard data and 30% judgement.</li>
<li>Find the minimally necessary data to support hypothesis</li>
<ul>
<li>No need to know whether a market segment is shrinking by 5% yearly or 20% yearly</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li>Use diagrams to take notes</li>
<ul>
<li>Don‚Äôt list, mind map instead. Two different sets of paper:</li>
<li>one focuses on case structure &amp; should be kept ultra neat. Exclusively tree diagrams and hypotheses. Even if you‚Äôre interrupted, this is your road map for you/interviewer to determine where you are.</li>
<ul>
<li>Graphically eliminate or verify branches here for the interviewer. One branch will visually remain at the end: The root of the problem.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<li>Two is a scratch pad and notes. Use this to determine whether you can eliminate a branch mathematically. This way, calculations can take pages but the roadmap will remain static. &nbsp;</li>
<li>Visible issue is often a symptom of an underlying problem, but you can‚Äôt just tell clients they‚Äôre wrong. You need severe justification beyond reasonable doubt.</li>
<li>Don‚Äôt just tell the answer; lead your listener to it logically</li>
</ul>
<li>Hypothesis: Apple should introduce the iPad.</li>
<ul>
<li>Customer - future market size <b>huge</b></li>
<ul>
<li>‚ÄúWho/where are your customers? What do they want to buy? How does competitors‚Äô customer information compare to yours?‚Äù</li>
</ul>
<li><b>Clients</b></li>
<ul>
<li>How do you operate in unique,&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<li>Competition - competitive <b>vacuum</b></li>
<li><b>Products</b></li>
<ul>
<li>‚ÄúHow do you differentiate your product?‚Äù</li>
</ul>
<li>Company - unique product design <b>advantage</b></li>
<li>ROI - potential financial <b>benefit far outweighs</b> R&amp;D costs</li>
</ul>
<li><b>Hypothesis: Client should enter the XYZ market.</b></li>
<ul>
<li>Consumer demand is strong, growing, and likely to remain growing.</li>
<ul>
<li>If after assessment the market is wavering, eliminate this branch.</li>
</ul>
<li>Client has a strong &amp; sustainable competitive advantage in XYZ market.</li>
<ul>
<li>Understand four key areas here:&nbsp;</li>
<ul>
<li>Customers(Who? Where? What demand? What do competitors know?)</li>
<li>Products (What offerings? How do they compete?)</li>
<li>Clients (What‚Äôs unique or unusual compared to competitors?)</li>
<li>Competition (Do competitors differentiate and how?)</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li>Competition in the market is modest &amp; can be beaten with modest effort</li>
<li>If a branch proves correct and consistent, continue. If not, reassess tree and hypothesis wholesale. If needed, narrow the hypothesis: ‚ÄúClient should enter the ABC (growing) segment of the XYZ market.‚Äù</li>
<ul>
<li>No single branch of the tree could lead you to this conclusion; you must factor multiple lines of logic</li>
<li>One market segment is growing and attractive.</li>
<ul>
<li>easy to prove/disprove with data</li>
</ul>
<li>Client has at least a modest competitive advantage here which has strengthened and will continue</li>
<ul>
<li>Tough to prove/disprove - Why is the segment growing? Is growth driven by certain customers/regions/product categories?&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<li>competition in this segment is weak or modest</li>
<ul>
<li>If competitors already occupy this segment, you may eliminate your hypothesis here</li>
</ul>
</ul>
</ul>
</ul>
<li><b>Synthesis</b></li>
<ul>
<li>For C-suite: at a $1b/yr company, $1500 in revenue/minute</li>
<li>What you discovered, what to do and three fact-backed reasons why</li>
<ul>
<li>Never more than three reasons</li>
</ul>
<li>Contextualize your recommendations within the business itself, as well as within the single one-time decision you‚Äôre advising on</li>
<li>Concise, detailed, analytical and action-oriented</li>
<li>START and END with what client SHOULD DO</li>
<li>Synthesize DURING the case:</li>
<ul>
<li>When transitioning between tree drilldown branches, state your conclusions and what they mean for the central case question</li>
<li>‚ÄúI can conclude sales aren‚Äôt causing the profit problem, because first, sales remain unchanged in the past year, and second, costs have gone up by $10m, accounting for 100% of the discrepancy. I‚Äôd like to analyze costs now to better understand why.‚Äù</li>
</ul>
</ul>
<li>Intuition can be correct but is usually impossible to prove correct; proof over instinct</li>
</ul>
<li>Frameworks:</li>
<ul>
<li>Profit/Loss = Revenue (#Purchased * price) - Cost(#produced * cost)</li>
<li>Competitive Landscape = Customers, Products, Client, Competitors</li>
<li>New market segment value = total MV * segment fraction / comp. Scape</li>
</ul></ul>"	business career economics formula

p6+*k(+F5w	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	List of Cognitive Biases, from Wikipedia	"<table><tbody><tr><td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_attribution_error"">Fundamental attribution error</a> (FAE, aka correspondence bias <sup><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_bias#cite_note-Baumeister-24"">[24]</a></sup>)
</td>
<td>Tendency to overemphasize personality-based explanations for 
behaviors observed in others. At the same time, individuals 
under-emphasize the role and power of situational influences on the same
 behavior. Edward E. Jones and Victor A. Harris' (1967)<sup><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_bias#cite_note-25"">[25]</a></sup>
 classic study illustrates the FAE. Despite being made aware that the 
target's speech direction (pro-Castro/anti-Castro) was assigned to the 
writer, participants ignored the situational pressures and attributed 
pro-Castro attitudes to the writer when the speech represented such 
attitudes.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Implicit_stereotype"">Implicit bias</a> (aka implicit stereotype, unconscious bias)
</td>
<td>Tendency to attribute positive or negative qualities to a group of 
individuals. It can be fully non-factual or be an abusive generalization
 of a frequent trait in a group to all individuals of that group.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Priming_(psychology)"">Priming bias</a>
</td>
<td>Tendency to be influenced by the first presentation of an issue to 
create our preconceived idea of it, which we then can adjust with later 
information.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Confirmation_bias"">Confirmation bias</a>
</td>
<td>Tendency to search for or interpret information in a way that 
confirms one's preconceptions, and discredit information that does not 
support the initial opinion.<sup><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_bias#cite_note-26"">[26]</a></sup> Related to the concept of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_dissonance"">cognitive dissonance</a>,
 in that individuals may reduce inconsistency by searching for 
information which reconfirms their views (Jermias, 2001, p.&nbsp;146).<sup><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_bias#cite_note-27"">[27]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Affinity bias
</td>
<td>Tendency to be favorably biased toward people most like ourselves.<sup><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_bias#cite_note-28"">[28]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-serving_bias"">Self-serving bias</a>
</td>
<td>Tendency to claim more responsibility for successes than for 
failures. It may also manifest itself as a tendency for people to 
evaluate ambiguous information in a way beneficial to their interests.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belief_bias"">Belief bias</a>
</td>
<td>Tendency to evaluate the logical strength of an argument based on 
current belief and perceived plausibility of the statement's conclusion.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Framing_effect_(psychology)"">Framing</a>
</td>
<td>Tendency to narrow the description of a situation in order to guide 
to a selected conclusion. The same primer can be framed differently and 
therefor lead to different conclusions.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindsight_bias"">Hindsight bias</a>
</td>
<td>Tendency to view past events as being predictable. Also called the ""I-knew-it-all-along"" effect.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embodied_cognition"">Embodied cognition</a>
</td>
<td>Tendency to have selectivity in perception, attention, decision 
making, and motivation based on the biological state of the body.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anchoring_(cognitive_bias)"">Anchoring bias</a>
</td>
<td>The inability of people to make appropriate adjustments from a 
starting point in response to a final answer. It can lead people to make
 sub-optimal decisions. Anchoring affects decision making in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negotiation"">negotiations</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medical_diagnosis"">medical diagnoses</a>, and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sentence_(law)"">judicial sentencing</a>.<sup><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_bias#cite_note-29"">[29]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Status_quo_bias"">Status quo bias</a>
</td>
<td>Tendency to hold to the current situation rather than an alternative situation, to avoid risk and loss (<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loss_aversion"">loss aversion</a>).<sup><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_bias#cite_note-30"">[30]</a></sup> In status quo bias, a decision-maker has the increased propensity to choose an option because it is the default option or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Status_quo"">status quo</a>. Has been shown to affect various important economic decisions, for example, a choice of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vehicle_insurance"">car insurance</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_utility"">electrical service</a>.<sup><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_bias#cite_note-31"">[31]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>

yBj;ItBKR$	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Modeling	"<div>The most common question on here seems to be how to get started so here's a post about everything you need to know, sorry if I missed anything.</div>
<div><b>How do I get an agency/how do I get scouted?</b></div>
<div>There are many ways to get scouted, it could be on the street or it could be by using an agencies social media scouting tags. For example, IMG scouts on social media using the hashtag (#WLYG &amp; #weloveyourgenes) you can also tag the accounts @/weloveyourgenes &amp; @/imgmodels on the posts.</div>
<div>Many agencies have this method of scouting online and you can go to their website to see if they offer it. If you get scouted this way make sure it is by the verified accounts, if you're unsure if it's a trusted account get in contact with the main page to ask. In most cases the agency will ask to see more photos/videos of you then ask for a zoom meeting (or in person if you are in the city you're applying to) the offer a contract if they like you. Don't be afraid to ask questions and have someone look over it for you before signing.</div>
<div>The most common method would be to apply to an agency through there website. You don't need professional photos for this, just follow the guidelines on the website and take them on a phone or digital camera. Do not wear makeup and pick an outfit that shows your figure. Skinny jeans and a tank top is always a good choice.</div>
<div>Finding a trust worthy agency is key. I recommend looking on models.com to find a reliable agency. They have agencies ranked from premium to basic, as well as what country/city they are located in. Many agencies take models from all over so don't be scared to apply if you do not live in the same place you are applying to. If there are no agencies near you, there is always the option to apply to a small boutique agency near you.</div>
<div><a href=""https://models.com/agencies/modeling/All/All"">https://models.com/agencies/modeling/All/All</a></div>
<div><b>What's a mother agency</b></div>
<div>A mother agency generally refers to the first agency you sign with. The goal of mother agencies is to help you develop as a model. They usually help get you scouted in different markets or bigger agencies. However, it all depends on your contract. If you have an exclusive contract with your mother agency you are usually not allowed to sign with other agencies in different markets. If you have a non-exclusive contract you are free to sign with other agencies on your own.</div>
<div><b>What do I pay to an agency</b></div>
<div>A trustworthy agency will never make you pay anything upfront, and many that do ask you to pay are scams. However, most agencies do take a commission fee out of your jobs, this can be anywhere from 0-30% (20% is most common). A lot of agencies do charge for website fees but this is usually under $100. Things such as hotels, flights, and model apartments also come from your paycheck. Comp cards are usually $1 and there are digital versions you can present. Test shoots can be free or they can cost money, it depends on who you're photographer is and your agent should always check with you beforehand if it's ok to spend money. Paychecks are not upfront and tend to be given months after a job; most agencies offer cash advances but this usually has a small interest rate.</div>
<div><b>Commercial vs High Fashion modeling</b></div>
<div>Anyone can be a commercial model, there are no standards. The work included in this type of modeling includes ecommerce, catalogues, tv ads, fit modeling, etc. It does not included runway work and editorials are rare. However, commercial modeling tends to pay much better than high fashion work.</div>
<div>High fashion modeling includes editorials, runway, etc. High fashion has very strict standards; models are expected to be 5'9+ and have measurements no larger than 36-25-36. The pay for high fashion modeling is usually not very high and a lot of work gets paid through exposure.</div>
<div><b>Model Apartments</b></div>
<div>Model apartments are what they sound like. Apartments with multiple models living in them at a time. Most model apartments have bunk beds for all the models living there. They are not free and usually cost anywhere from $20-$70 a night. Generally this comes from your paycheck and model apartments tend to get a lot of models in debt to their agency. If possible try to find an apartment and room with other models, it is cheaper and cleaner that way.</div>
<div><b>Social Media</b></div>
<div>You do not need social media to become a model but it is looked at by both agencies and clients. A agency may sign a model with more followers and a client may pick a model with a higher amount of followers. Your social media should generally have at least some of the work you've done on there.</div>
<div><b>Casting Calls</b></div>
<div>This is basically an ""audition"", bring your comp card and show up early. Wear little to no makeup and once again skinny jeans and a tank is a good outfit.</div>
<div><b>Do I have what it takes?</b></div>
<div>It all depends on the agency and what they are looking for. An agency may pass on you while another may love your look. Focus on getting an agency that truly believes in you over a big agency.</div>
<div><br></div>
<div><br></div>
<div>Some good headshots, professional look, athletic and casual looks are usually a necessity.</div>
<div><br></div>

<div><br></div>
<div>Chances are your 9-5 will always make more than modeling plus you get very good benefits. Save at least two years of rent and a bit more. You have to pay for EVERYTHING it's not glamorous. Clients will sometimes pay for hotels and flights but that is rare, usually you pay out of future paychecks. There's also website fees you need to pay. Pay is inconsistent and you can be getting payment for a job months later and jobs aren't consistent. Even when you do get paid the amount is always lower due to commissions and fees that go to your agency. My agency offers cash advances with fees.</div>
<div><br></div>
<div>If you're going to be staying in a model apartment you need to pay for that too. You'll likely be in debt to your agency when starting out. One of the model apartments I was in was $90 a day (roughly $2700 monthly) with each room having 2/3 bunk beds with strict rules. Agencies pay a small fee of like $5000 monthly for the apartment and with multiple girls living there at the same time they are making huge profits. It is not free to stay there, agencies will take your paychecks to pay for the apartments</div>

Best way to do it as a side job though is not to be signed with an agency as they will have you going to castings randomly throughout the week and random times. So it'll be hard to have your primary job and be signed. Freelancing is your best bet. I am signed in NYC and I quickly realized it was nearly impossible to call off for my 9-5 as I could never anticipate when my agent would put me to work.</div>

JK/*fp2v,p	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	"Malcolm Gladwell's ""Outliers"""	"<div><strong>Introduction:</strong>&nbsp;</div><div>""Outliers: The Story of Success"" by Malcolm Gladwell is a deep dive into what constitutes success and how it is often a product of more than just individual ambition and intelligence. Gladwell posits that success is a complex concoction of opportunities, cultural legacy, and the time we are born into. He challenges the conventional wisdom that success is solely a result of personal effort, instead highlighting the importance of external factors that contribute to an individual's ability to succeed.</div><div><br></div><div><strong>Movement 1: The Role of Opportunity and Timing:</strong>&nbsp;</div><div>Gladwell introduces the concept of the ""Matthew Effect,"" where he discusses how small advantages can accumulate over time, leading to significant disparities in success. He illustrates this with the ""10,000-Hour Rule,"" suggesting that true expertise in any skill is the result of approximately 10,000 hours of practice, an opportunity that not everyone can access. This rule is exemplified by the Beatles, who performed lengthy gigs in Hamburg, and Bill Gates, who had unique access to a computer at a time when such access was rare.</div><div><br></div><div><strong>Movement 2: The Trouble with Geniuses:</strong>&nbsp;</div><div>In his examination of intelligence, Gladwell presents the ""Trouble with Geniuses,"" where he argues that beyond a certain IQ threshold, the predictive value of intelligence on success is limited. He discusses the stories of Christopher Langan and Robert Oppenheimer, showing that social savvy and practical intelligence are just as important as raw intellect, if not more so.</div><div><br></div><div><strong>Movement 3: Cultural Legacy:</strong>&nbsp;</div><div>Gladwell explores the ""Ethnic Theory of Plane Crashes,"" where he attributes differing cultural legacies of communication to the safety records of various airlines. He also discusses how cultural legacies affect achievement in education, as seen in the rigorous work ethic derived from the cultural traditions of rice farming in Asian countries.</div><div><br></div><div><strong>Conclusion:</strong>&nbsp;</div><div>Gladwell's ""Outliers"" is a compelling narrative that shifts the focus from individual attributes to a broader sociocultural context. It underscores the importance of acknowledging the external factors that contribute to success, such as the time and place of one's birth, cultural legacies, and access to opportunities. By understanding these factors, we can better appreciate the complexities behind success and the potential for cultivating it in others.</div><h3>10 Important Case Studies/Examples</h3><ol><li><div><strong>The Roseto Mystery:</strong> A small town in Pennsylvania with a remarkably low rate of heart disease, which Gladwell attributes to the close-knit community and social support, rather than diet or genetics. (Pages 10-17)</div></li><li><div><strong>The 10,000-Hour Rule:</strong> The Beatles and Bill Gates serve as examples of how extensive practice time leads to mastery in their fields. The Beatles played long hours in Hamburg, while Gates had unique access to a computer during adolescence. (Pages 7)</div></li><li><div><strong>The Matthew Effect:</strong> Gladwell discusses how small initial advantages can lead to success, such as Canadian hockey players born earlier in the year being more successful due to age and maturity advantages in youth leagues. (Pages 6)</div></li><li><div><strong>The Trouble with Geniuses, Part 1 &amp; 2:</strong> The contrasting stories of Christopher Langan, a man with a high IQ who struggled with practical aspects of success, and Robert Oppenheimer, who despite setbacks, used his practical intelligence to achieve prominence. (Pages 7-8)</div></li><li><div><strong>The Three Lessons of Joe Flom:</strong> The story of a successful lawyer that illustrates the importance of being in the right place at the right time, with the right cultural background. (Pages 7)</div></li><li><div><strong>Harlan, Kentucky:</strong> Gladwell examines the culture of honor in the American South and how it relates to higher rates of violence and the historical legacy of the region. (Pages 8)</div></li><li><div><strong>The Ethnic Theory of Plane Crashes:</strong> An examination of how power distance in different cultures affects communication in the cockpit and can lead to disasters. (Pages 8)</div></li><li><div><strong>Rice Paddies and Math Tests:</strong> The connection between the labor-intensive rice paddies of Asian cultures and the strong work ethic that contributes to success in mathematics. (Pages 8)</div></li><li><div><strong>Marita's Bargain:</strong> The story of a student from a disadvantaged background who succeeds through the opportunity provided by the KIPP academy, a school with longer hours and a demanding curriculum. (Pages 8)</div></li><li><div><strong>A Jamaican Story:</strong> Gladwell's own family background, illustrating how cultural legacies and personal histories shape opportunities and success. (Pages 8)</div></li></ol><h3>5 Bottom-Line Pieces of Actionable Advice</h3><ol><li><div><strong>Seek and Create Opportunities:</strong> Success often comes from having the chance to put in the ""10,000 hours"" of practice in a chosen field. Seek out environments that offer these opportunities or create them if they do not exist.</div></li><li><div><strong>Understand the Importance of Timing:</strong> Be aware of how birth dates and historical periods can affect opportunities for success. Timing can be crucial, so look for trends and patterns that might offer advantages.</div></li><li><div><strong>Value Practical Intelligence:</strong> Develop social savvy and negotiation skills. Intelligence is not just about IQ but also about being able to navigate and negotiate complex social environments.</div></li><li><div><strong>Acknowledge the Power of Legacy:</strong> Recognize and understand your cultural background and how its values and norms might influence your behavior and success.</div></li><li><div><strong>Foster Community and Support Networks:</strong> Like the residents of Roseto, build strong social ties and support networks. Community and relationships can be a powerful buffer against stress and a catalyst for success.</div></li></ol>"	book career history
He}=q{b$vn	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	How to Grow Your Career in AI - Andrew Ng handbook for Fourthbrain.ai	"**5-Paragraph Summary**<br><br>**Introduction:**<br>Andrew Ng's guide to career growth in AI emphasizes the transformative power of AI, likening coding for AI to a new form of literacy. He draws parallels between historical literacy and the current trajectory of AI, suggesting that just as reading and writing became fundamental skills for societal advancement, so too will the ability to code and work with AI (Page 4).<br><br>**First Major Movement:**<br>Ng outlines a three-step framework for career growth in AI: learning foundational skills, working on projects, and finding a job. He stresses that learning is a lifelong process, especially in a rapidly evolving field like AI. Foundational skills include understanding machine learning models and core concepts, deep learning, relevant mathematics, and software development. Ng advises that organized courses are often the most efficient way to master these skills (Pages 5-10).<br><br>**Second Major Movement:**<br>The author discusses the importance of scoping successful AI projects. He provides a five-step process for identifying and developing AI solutions to real-world problems. This involves understanding business problems, brainstorming AI solutions, assessing feasibility and value, determining milestones, and budgeting for resources. Ng emphasizes the iterative nature of this process and encourages seeking impactful projects even without deep domain expertise (Pages 14-16).<br><br>**Third Major Movement:**<br>Ng touches on the role of math in AI, suggesting that while a deep understanding of mathematics can be beneficial, especially for debugging and optimizing algorithms, the necessity varies with the maturity of the technology. He encourages learning driven by curiosity and the pursuit of continuous learning to stay abreast of the rapidly changing field (Pages 12-13).<br><br>**Conclusion:**<br>In conclusion, Ng's book provides a comprehensive roadmap for individuals looking to grow their careers in AI. The focus is on continuous learning, practical project experience, and the strategic application of AI to solve meaningful problems. He advocates for a balanced approach to learning technical skills, understanding the math behind AI, and the importance of community and collaboration in this journey (Pages 1-17).<br><br>**10 Bullet Points Highlighting Important Case Studies/Examples:**<br><br>1. **AI as the New Electricity:**<br>&nbsp;&nbsp; Let reader get bearings within topic you're addressing
""The best theses are narrowly circumscribed""
Contribute to a larger dialogue. Don't end the debate; you'd be lucky to carry it a step forward. And narrow that debate to key points.
Your thesis can be: 'X's claim Y generates problem Z.' You don't need to prove that X can't solve Z, but now you've struck a blow and demonstrated cost.
You might want to argue X's claim Y FAILS for reason Z. But even with a sound thesis, X could still defend Y.
If your paper's not interesting, your thesis is probably too narrow.</div>
<h2>For classes, target a particular argument rather than a particular thesis.</h2>
<div>Other theses:
""In this paper I offer an additional reason to accept Y: Z.""
""Claim X generates problem Y. We make progress on Y if we bring Z to bear.""
""Two interpretations are possible. I argue that this one is better.""</div>
<h2>Body</h2>
<div>reconstruct arguments as charitably as you can - otherwise your objections will ring hollow
You're writing to someone who dropped after the first day of your class. Don't catch them up on the course, but situate them in the debate.
DON'T sacrifice depth for breadth
Make sure your argument actually supports your thesis</div>
<h2>Rubric</h2>
<div>Organization - structure, signposting, follow-through
Intelligibility - clear, coherent, concise, no words out of place, no technicalities unexplained, precision with pronouns &amp; synonyms
Cogency - does it have a thesis? How successful of one? Have you backed it up and avoided leaps in reasoning? Have you been sensitive/charitable to others in argument?
Limit hedging terms like ""seems"" or ""may""</div>
<div>Be honest about your limitations if you intend for your readers to come around to your perspective. <h2>Pitching</h2>

Kdsn[1j7,K	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	ü™ûEmpathy/Understanding Others	"<div>Your difficulties are not relative. Your problems are not more difficult than anyone else‚Äôs. Let‚Äôs not constantly compare our problems to each other and judge our difficulties based on others‚Äô and instead commiserate on the fact that everyone has difficulties. Everyone has hard. There is no harder when it comes to problems in life, only hard.</div>
<div>If you want someone to be real with you, they need to know that you suffer too. Be real and unapologetic with truth. Apologize for what you have done, not for who you are.</div>
<div>The worst thing to tell someone who is depressed or even just sad is</div>
<div>‚ÄúLife is great! Enjoy it!‚Äù or</div>
<div>‚ÄúYou have it pretty good, what are you sad for?‚Äù or</div>
<div>‚ÄúI was sad once, I know what you‚Äôre feeling.‚Äù or</div>
<div>‚ÄúIf I was able to recover, so can you.‚Äù</div>
<div>Nothing about depression is their fault. You know what pain is, but you don‚Äôt know how it feels to them. Do not compare your illness to theirs.</div>
<div>Some signs of depression are:</div>
<div>hopelessness</div>
<div>helplessness</div>
<div>social withdrawal</div>
<div>loss of interest in life</div>
<div>Here are some simple things that you can do to help someone who is depressed:</div>
<ul>
<li>Listen to understand.</li>
<li>Do not argue, blame, or tell the person you know how they feel.</li>
<li>Ask how they are feeling</li>
<li>Do not be judgemental</li>
<li>Do not make them feel guilty about the effect it is having on others</li>
<li>Let the person who self-harms know that you want to listen to them and hear how they are feeling when they feel ready and able to talk.</li>
<li>When they do discuss it with you be compassionate and respect what the person is telling you, even though you may not understand or find it difficult to accept what they are doing.</li>
<li>Do not give ultimatums such as 'If you don't stop self-harming you have to move out'. This is not helpful and it won't work.</li>
<li>Understand that it is a long and hard journey to stop self-harming. Be aware that someone will only stop self-harming when they feel ready and able to do so.</li>
<li>Sometimes it is best to confront the depressed. Ask, ‚ÄúOthers in similar circumstances have thought about ending their lives. Have you thought about it?‚Äù</li>
</ul>
<div>The large majority of teens who self-harm do not do it for attention or with the intent of suicide; most often it acts as a release for stress or a coping mechanism for frustration with the misunderstanding of others or a lack of distinct pain in one‚Äôs life. Only 25% of those who self harm want to commit suicide. Don‚Äôt tell the person not to do it, you need to respect their right to harm themselves. Present alternatives for coping like mindfulness, sports, or talking to supports, and, if nothing else, make the harm as safe as possible- Make sure they are using clean blades, clean the wound after, etc. Most importantly, be unconditionally supportive and non-judgemental. Be ready to help them decrease the behavior when they feel ready.</div>"	behavior formula persuasion psychology sociology technique

KPG$S!)*~k	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	üí≥Credit	"<ul>
<li>Your credit score or FICO score will improve if you have a higher credit-to-utilization ratio i.e. if you‚Äôre using less than your limit. However, credit limits are bumped to increase spending- maintain frugality. Keep your utilization percentage low but above 0. 3:</li>
<li>Make credit card payments more than once a month so that your balance never gets too high.</li>
<li>If you have more than one credit card, another good way you might lower your utilization is to use multiple cards each month. This results in various cards with low credit utilization rather than one with high utilization.</li>
<li>Lastly, you could try to increase your available credit. If your income has increased, if you've maintained an amazing credit history or if you have little debt, it doesn't hurt to ask for a credit limit increase. Just remember that this can sometimes result in a <a href=""http://www.creditkarma.com/article/hard_inquiries_and_soft_inquiries"">hard inquiry</a> on your credit. If you lack excellent credit, you may want to consider opening a <a href=""http://www.creditkarma.com/creditcards/secured_cards"">secured credit card</a> and adding to its security deposit over time.‚Äô</li>
<li>You do not have to carry a credit card balance or pay interest every month to show credit card utilization. Even if you <a href=""https://www.creditkarma.com/article/when-should-i-pay-my-credit-cards"">pay your credit card balances</a> in full every month, simply using your card is enough to show activity.</li>
<li>Experts recommend keeping your credit card utilization below 30 percent on each card and collectively. This shows lenders that you know how to spend responsibly and can help your credit score. 1% to 9% is a green light for creditors, 10% to 30% is average but will keep you from the top scores and best rates. 30%+ is not good. However, creditors also care about the total dollar amount of your available credit, so if you have a low credit limit, it usually is not a big deal if your credit card utilization rate is slightly higher than recommended.</li>
<li>Although high credit utilization can be detrimental to credit scores, keeping credit utilization at 0 percent is not recommended either. Creditors want to see people who use their credit, but are able to manage it responsibly.</li></ul>"	career economics finance

IE,p9,8c=Q	Basic	Anthro (Psych/Soc/Econ/Health)	Jim Simons / Renaissance Technologies	"<div>James Harris Simons founded Renaissance Technologies in 1982, creating what became the most successful quantitative trading firm in history. The firm's flagship Medallion Fund has averaged approximately 66% annual returns (before fees) over multiple decades, significantly outperforming all other investment vehicles.</div>
<div>Key Technical Innovations:</div>
<div>Simons pioneered the use of mathematical and statistical patterns in market data. His key innovation was applying pattern recognition techniques from codebreaking (his previous work at the IDA during the Cold War) to financial markets. The core insight was that market inefficiencies could be identified through advanced statistical analysis of historical price data.</div>
<div>The Technical Framework:</div>
<ol>
<li>Data Collection: Renaissance built one of the largest financial databases in the world, collecting and cleaning tick-by-tick data across multiple markets</li>
<li>Pattern Recognition: They developed algorithms to identify statistical patterns that had predictive power over short time horizons</li>
<li>Execution: High-frequency trading systems were built to capitalize on these patterns before they disappeared</li>
<li>Risk Management: Complex statistical arbitrage strategies were employed to maintain market neutrality</li>
</ol>
<div>Modern Application Principles:</div>
<div>For a software engineer looking to apply these concepts today:</div>
<div>First, focus on data infrastructure. Any successful quantitative strategy requires pristine historical data and reliable real-time feeds. This means building robust data pipelines and validation systems.</div>
<div>Second, understand that simple patterns are likely arbitraged away. Modern strategies require analyzing multiple data sources simultaneously and finding complex interactions. This suggests using advanced machine learning techniques rather than simple statistical measures.</div>
<div>Third, execution speed matters more than ever. Success requires both identifying patterns and acting on them faster than competitors. This means optimization at every level - from algorithm design to network latency.</div>
<div>Fourth, risk management is paramount. Simons' success came not just from identifying opportunities, but from building a portfolio of uncorrelated strategies that could perform well in different market conditions.</div>
<div>Important Caveats:
The specific algorithms used by Renaissance remain proprietary and attempting to reverse engineer them would be speculative. Additionally, the resources required to compete in modern markets are substantial - Renaissance employs over 100 PhDs and has spent decades building their infrastructure.</div>"	career economics finance history

The course advises tailoring presentations to different audiences (from C-level executives to direct team members), always focusing on user needs, supporting data, and demonstrable measurements of success. ""Make sure the rest is in an appendix, and include all of your facts,"" is a practical tip for executive presentations. <div>#Form</div>
<div>##hip thrusts -
use elbows to raise yourself
come up hiigher on the bench than you think you need, mid scapula
at bottom of thrust shins should be vertical, keep your head stationary
head to sternum does not move - stay looking straiight forward
Use only glutes and abs, lead with pelvis, deactivate the erectors
don't rock &amp; don't arch your chest</div>
<div>##Goblet Squats
feet shoulder width apart
elbows between knees, don't let knees pass feet
push knees out through max activation, rather than forward</div>
<div>#Deadlifts
##Conventional
arms just outside knees, keep chest up &amp; look forward
lower it as much as you can before bending knees
keep back absolutely straight, never round your back, esp lumbar
##stiff leg
toward bottom of exercise, you let the bar come forward
don't bend knees as much, swing forward more</div>

<div>##hip/back extension -
set pad right below pubic bone
feet 45 degrees,
keep top spine curled and chin to chest, don't extend past horizontal,
keep activation in glutes, shut off erectors completely</div>
<div>Maximize fat loss prevent muscle loss</div>
<ol>
<li>energy deficit caloric</li>
<li>progressive resistance exercises</li>
<li>appropriate deficit size - 0.5-1% of bodyweight lost per week</li>
<li>More than 2g protein/kg body fat</li>
<li>1g protein per centimeter in height</li>
<li>appropriate volume of cardio - not right before weights, lower impact is ideal, no more than half of time spent weight training is cardio
no more than 30mins low intensity per day, no more than 1-2 sessions high intensity per week
mostly single-ingredient food items, no hyperpalatable foods, need to have cheats with most healthy
duration of diet + diligence is only way to scale beach lean vs. competition</li>
</ol>
<div>WHAT YOU NEED TO DO FOR YOUR BELLY FAT
don't fall victim to shiny object syndrome:
get very excited in beginning
rely too much on motivation
underestimate difficulty of journey
quit
Choice architecture intervention - altering physical and social environments /micro-environments to cue healthier behavior</div>
<div>##weight maintenance
dietary adherence is terrible across the board</div>
<ol>
<li>cognitive restraint - calorie counting, fasting, points, restriction</li>
<li>regular progress tracking - provide feedback for yourself</li>
<li>regular exercise</li>
<li>structured programs</li>
<li>low-recency - valuing long-term data more than short-term data</li>
</ol>
